Actual and Estimated Compounds in the Definition of a Proposition
Author extends the definition to cover estimated compounds and examples of certain truth or falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is an actual or estimated compound according to the definition.

Core Idea
The definition covers not only explicit propositions but also those where parts are implied.

The passage extends the definition of a proposition to include not just fully explicit compounds like 'Zayd stood', but also estimated compounds like 'I stand' where the subject pronoun is implied. Understanding this scope is important for recognizing that all truth-apt expressions are included.

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit part of a compound proposition
Look for the subject and predicate that are actually written. In 'Zayd stood', both are explicit.
2
Recognize estimated elements
In Arabic, pronouns can be implied. For example, in 'I stand' (أقوم), the subject 'I' is implied but effectively present.
The implied pronoun is considered part of the proposition.
3
Extend to entire estimated propositions
Sometimes the whole proposition is estimated, as in responses like 'yes' (نعم) or 'indeed' (بلى) which imply a full proposition.
The definition still includes these because they are truth-apt in context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ : المُرَكَّبَ تَحْقِيقاً كَـ: «قَامَ زَيْدٌ ، وَتَقْدِيراً نَحْوُ : «أَقُومُ»، فَإِنَّ ضَمِيرَهُ المُسْتَيَرَ فِي حُكْمِ المَذْكُورِ، وَقَدْ تُقَدَّرُ القَضِيَّةُ كُلُّهَا كَالوَاقِعَةِ بَعْدَ «نَعَمْ، وَبَلَى». وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ أَيْضاً : الخَبَرَ الَّذِي قُطِعَ بِصِدْقِهِ : لِخَارِج عَنْ حَقِيقَتِهِ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى المُخْبِرِ؛ كَأَخْبَارِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَأَخْبَارِ رُسُلِهِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ؛ لِاسْتِحَالَةِ الكَذِبِ عَلَيْهِمْ، أَوْ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى خُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ؛ نَحْرُ : الوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَيْنِ». - وَكَذَا مَا قُطِعَ بِكَذِبِهِ : لِخَارِج عَنْ حَقِيقَتِهِ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «الوَاحِدُ رُبُعُ الاثْنَيْنِ، وَكَخَبَرِ مُسَيْلِمَةَ بِأَنَّهُ نَبِيٌّ ، وَالدَّجَّالَ بِأَنَّهُ إِلَهُ، فَإِنَّهُ مَقْطُوعٌ بِكَذِبِهِ؛ لِخُصُوص المَادَّةِ؛ لِقِيَامِ البُرْهَانِ القَاطِعِ عَلَى كَذِبِهِ ، لَا لِذَاتِ الخَبَرِ، وَلَا لِخُصُوص المُخْبِـ بالكَسْرِ - كَمَا قِيْلَ ; إِذْ لَوْ أَخْبَرَ مُسَيْلِمَةَ بِقِيَامٍ زَيْدٍ لَمْ يُقْطَعْ بِكَذِبِهِ .
And the definition includes the compound actually, like 'Zayd stood', and estimated, like 'I stand', for its implied pronoun is in the status of the mentioned. And sometimes the entire proposition is estimated like that which occurs after 'yes' (na'am) and 'indeed' (bala). And the definition also includes the report that is known to be true due to something external from its essence, considering the informant, like the reports of Allah Most High and the reports of His messengers (peace and blessings be upon them) due to the impossibility of lying from them, or considering the particular matter, like 'one is half of two'. And likewise what is known to be false due to something external from its essence for the particular matter, like 'one is a quarter of two', and like the report of Musaylima that he is a prophet, and the Dajjal that he is a god, for it is known to be false due to the particular matter, because of the conclusive proof of its falsehood, not because of the essence of the report, nor because of the particular informant (as it is said), for if Musaylima reported that Zayd is standing, it would not be known to be false.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the definition of a proposition. It explains that the definition covers not only explicitly stated compound propositions like 'Zayd stood' (قام زيد), but also estimated compounds like 'I stand' (أقوم) where the pronoun is implicit but effectively present. Moreover, entire propositions can be estimated, such as the response after 'yes' or 'indeed' in dialogue, where the proposition is understood from context. The passage then addresses a nuance: some propositions are known to be true or false with certainty, but this certainty comes from external factors, not from the proposition itself. For example, reports from Allah or the prophets are certain truth due to the impossibility of lying from them. Mathematical statements like 'one is half of two' are certain truth due to the nature of mathematics. Conversely, statements like 'one is a quarter of two' are certain falsehood due to mathematical impossibility. Also, reports from false prophets like Musaylima claiming prophethood are certain falsehood because of conclusive proof (burhan) against them. However, this certainty is not due to the essence of the report or the personal identity of the informant per se, because if Musaylima reported something ordinary like 'Zayd is standing', it would not be certain falsehood. Thus, the definition remains intact: all propositions, whether certain or uncertain, are included as long as they are truth-apt by themselves.

Key Terms
مركب تحقيقاً
مركب تقديراً
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an estimated compound?
A) 'زيد قائم' (Zayd is standing)
B) 'أقوم' (I stand)
C) 'السماء زرقاء' (The sky is blue)
D) 'العالم حادث' (The world is originated)

Key Takeaway
The definition of a proposition includes both actual and estimated compounds, ensuring all truth-apt expressions are covered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following are actual or estimated compounds: 'زيد قائم' (Zayd is standing), 'أقوم' (I stand), 'نعم' (yes) in response to 'هل قام زيد؟'. Why is 'نعم' considered a proposition according to this definition?

Certain Truth and Falsehood: External Factors in Propositional Evaluation
Author extends the definition to cover estimated compounds and examples of certain truth or falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between certain truth/falsehood due to external factors and the inherent truth-aptness of a proposition.

Core Idea
Certainty of truth or falsehood does not remove a proposition from the definition; it only actualizes its truth value.

The passage addresses a nuance: some propositions are known to be true or false with certainty because of external factors like divine revelation or mathematical proof. However, this certainty does not exclude them from the definition; it merely means their truth value is fully actualized. Understanding this helps avoid the misconception that certain propositions are not 'real' propositions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between the proposition itself and external factors
A proposition like 'one is half of two' is true by mathematical necessity, but the truth is not part of the proposition's essence; it is due to the specific content (al-madda).
2
Recognize certainty from the informant's trustworthiness
Reports from Allah and the prophets are certainly true because of the impossibility of lying from them, not because of the proposition's content.
This certainty is external to the proposition.
3
Apply to falsehood
Propositions known to be false with certainty, like 'one is a quarter of two' or 'Musaylima is a prophet', are false due to conclusive proof against them, not due to the essence of the report.
If Musaylima reported an ordinary fact, it would not be certainly false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ : المُرَكَّبَ تَحْقِيقاً كَـ: «قَامَ زَيْدٌ ، وَتَقْدِيراً نَحْوُ : «أَقُومُ»، فَإِنَّ ضَمِيرَهُ المُسْتَيَرَ فِي حُكْمِ المَذْكُورِ، وَقَدْ تُقَدَّرُ القَضِيَّةُ كُلُّهَا كَالوَاقِعَةِ بَعْدَ «نَعَمْ، وَبَلَى». وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ أَيْضاً : الخَبَرَ الَّذِي قُطِعَ بِصِدْقِهِ : لِخَارِج عَنْ حَقِيقَتِهِ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى المُخْبِرِ؛ كَأَخْبَارِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَأَخْبَارِ رُسُلِهِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ؛ لِاسْتِحَالَةِ الكَذِبِ عَلَيْهِمْ، أَوْ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى خُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ؛ نَحْرُ : الوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَيْنِ». - وَكَذَا مَا قُطِعَ بِكَذِبِهِ : لِخَارِج عَنْ حَقِيقَتِهِ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «الوَاحِدُ رُبُعُ الاثْنَيْنِ، وَكَخَبَرِ مُسَيْلِمَةَ بِأَنَّهُ نَبِيٌّ ، وَالدَّجَّالَ بِأَنَّهُ إِلَهُ، فَإِنَّهُ مَقْطُوعٌ بِكَذِبِهِ؛ لِخُصُوص المَادَّةِ؛ لِقِيَامِ البُرْهَانِ القَاطِعِ عَلَى كَذِبِهِ ، لَا لِذَاتِ الخَبَرِ، وَلَا لِخُصُوص المُخْبِـ بالكَسْرِ - كَمَا قِيْلَ ; إِذْ لَوْ أَخْبَرَ مُسَيْلِمَةَ بِقِيَامٍ زَيْدٍ لَمْ يُقْطَعْ بِكَذِبِهِ .
And the definition includes the compound actually, like 'Zayd stood', and estimated, like 'I stand', for its implied pronoun is in the status of the mentioned. And sometimes the entire proposition is estimated like that which occurs after 'yes' (na'am) and 'indeed' (bala). Four Types of Propositions (Akhbar) and the Refutation of a Fifth
Author enumerates four kinds of propositions based on possibility or certainty of truth/falsehood and their sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of propositions based on what determines their truth or falsehood, and explain why a fifth category proposed by al-Yusi is rejected.

Core Idea
Propositions are classified by what determines their truth or falsehood: neither, the informant, or the content.

In this passage, the commentator explains the division of reports (akhbar) into exactly four kinds, contrary to the claim of al-Yusi who argued for a fifth. Understanding these categories is essential for evaluating the certainty of statements in Islamic theology and law, where the reliability of the informant and the nature of the content play key roles. Let's unpack the four types and the critical refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Read the enumeration carefully. The first category is ordinary reports that could be true or false. The second is reports from an infallible source (truth determined by the informant). The third is reports whose truth is determined by their content (like mathematical truths). The fourth is reports whose falsehood is determined by their content (like contradictions).
Notice that each category is defined by a different factor: the informant's character, the logical necessity, or lack thereof.
2
Understand the basis for each category
The first type is 'muhtamil li al-sidq wa al-kidhb mutlaqan' (absolutely possible). The second is 'muta'ayyan sidquhu bi al-nazar ila al-mukhbir' (truth determined by the informant). The third is by the matter (madda), and the fourth is by the object reported (mukhbar bihi).
The second type requires the informant to be ma'sum (infallible), like a prophet or Allah.
3
Analyze the critical refinement
The commentator explicitly rejects a fifth category: reports whose falsehood is determined solely by the informant (like a known liar always lying). The reason given is that there is no textual evidence from the Shari'ah that any informant always lies; even a known liar can sometimes tell the truth.
This is a logical and empirical argument: determination of falsehood requires a universal pattern, which is not established for any human.
4
Apply the classification to examples
Practice classifying: 'Zayd is standing' (type 1), 'The Prophet said: the world is temporally originated' (type 2), 'One is half of two' (type 3), 'One is half of three' (type 4).
Ensure you can justify the classification based on the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقسام الخبر على ما ذكر الشَّارح أربعة فقط، لا خمسة خلافاً لليوسي:

1 - ما يحتمل الصدق والكذب مطلقاً ; كخبر من ليس معصوماً بنحو : «قيام زيد».

2 - وما يحتملهما لذاته وتعيّن صدقه ; نظراً للخارج من مخبر كخبر من يستحيل عليه الكذب. 3 - أو نظراً لخصوص المادة ; نحو: الواحد نصف الاثنين»، و: «الأربعة زوج». 4 - أو تعيَّن كذبه نظراً إلى المخبر به .

وليس عندنا ما هو مقطوع بكذبه نظراً إلى المخبر; إذ لم يرد نص مِنَ الشَّرع أنَّ كلَّ ما أخبر به -
Thus the divisions of the report according to what the commentator mentioned are only four, not five, contrary to al-Yusi: 1. What bears truth and falsehood absolutely, like the report of one who is not infallible, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'. 2. And what bears them by itself but its truth is determined considering the external, due to the informant, like the report of one from whom lying is impossible. 3. Or considering the particular matter, like 'one is half of two' and 'four is even'. 4. Or its falsehood is determined considering the object reported (al-mukhbar bihi). And we do not have something that is determined false considering the informant, because there is no text from the Shari'ah that everything that is reported by —
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of propositions (akhbar) based on their relation to truth and falsehood. The commentator states that there are exactly four kinds, rejecting a fifth categorization proposed by Shaykh al-Yusi. The four kinds are: (1) propositions that are simply possible (true or false) without determination, like any ordinary report from a non-infallible person. (2) Propositions that are inherently possible but are known to be true because the informant is incapable of lying, such as reports from Allah or the prophets. (3) Propositions whose truth is determined by their subject matter, like logical or mathematical truths (e.g., one is half of two). (4) Propositions whose falsehood is determined by the subject matter, like 'one is a quarter of two' or the false claims of Musaylima to prophethood, which are demonstrably false. The commentator then notes that there is no category where falsehood is determined solely by the informant (as al-Yusi claimed), because there is no textual evidence that any particular informant always lies; even a known liar can sometimes tell the truth. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
خبر
معصوم
مادة
مخبر به

Key Takeaway
The classification of propositions into four types helps determine the certainty of a report, and no additional category based solely on the informant's untruthfulness is valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Proposition: Inherent Capacity for Truth and Falsity
Commentator clarifies the definition of proposition and its scope including implicit and known statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a statement qualifies as a proposition based on its inherent capacity for truth and falsity, even when its truth value is known with certainty.

Core Idea
A proposition is defined by its essence, not by external certainty.

What makes a statement a proposition (qadiyya)? Is it the possibility of doubt, or something deeper? The classical logicians defined it as a composite statement that inherently admits truth and falsity. This definition includes even statements known to be certainly true or false, because the capacity is in the statement itself, not in our knowledge. Let's explore how the commentator clarifies this by adding the crucial phrase 'by itself' (li-dhatihi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition in the definition
The definition includes 'li-dhatihi' (by itself). This excludes relations that admit truth/falsity accidentally, such as constructive (insha'iyya) and incomplete (naqisa) relations.
The commentator explicitly states that constructive and incomplete relations admit truth/falsity but that is outside their concepts.
2
Distinguish between inherent capacity and external certainty
The passage lists examples known true or false from external factors: God's reports, mathematical truths, false prophecies. Recognise that these are still propositions because the capacity is inherent.
The certainty comes from outside the statement's essence, not from the statement itself.
3
Test the definition on examples
Apply the definition to 'the one is half of two' (known true) and 'the one is a quarter of two' (known false). Both are propositions because they inherently admit truth/falsity; external proof doesn't change that.
4
Apply to the case of impossible falsehood (God's reports)
Even though lying is impossible for Allah and His messengers, the report still has the inherent capacity for truth/falsity in its essence. The impossibility is external (due to the informant's attribute).
The commentator emphasises this to show the definition's scope.
5
Check your understanding with a non-proposition
A command like 'Stand up!' (qum) does not admit truth or falsity; it is not a proposition. Commands are not subject to truth evaluation.
The passage does not mention commands, but from the definition, they are excluded.

تَحْتَمِلُ النِّسْبَةُ الْإِنْشَائِيَّةُ وَالنَّاقِصَةُ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ، وَذَلِكَ خَارِجٌ عَنْ مَفْهُومَيْهِمَا، وَلِذَلِكَ زَادَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ قَوْلَهُ: «لِذَاتِهِ». وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ: الْمُرَكَّبَ تَحْقِيقاً كَـ: «قَامَ زَيْدٌ»، وَتَقْدِيراً نَحْوُ: «أَقُومُ»، فَإِنَّ ضَمِيرَهُ الْمُسْتَتِرَ فِي حُكْمِ الْمَذْكُورِ، وَقَدْ تُقَدَّرُ الْقَضِيَّةُ كُلُّهَا كَالْوَاقِعَةِ بَعْدَ «نَعَمْ» وَ«بَلَى». وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ أَيْضاً: الْخَبَرَ الَّذِي قُطِعَ بِصِدْقِهِ لِخَارِجٍ عَنْ حَقِيقَتِهِ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى الْمُخْبِرِ؛ كَأَخْبَارِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَأَخْبَارِ رُسُلِهِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ؛ لِاسْتِحَالَةِ الْكَذِبِ عَلَيْهِمْ، أَوْ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى خُصُوصِ الْمَادَّةِ؛ نَحْوُ: «الْوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَيْنِ». وَكَذَا مَا قُطِعَ بِكَذِبِهِ لِخَارِجٍ عَنْ حَقِيقَتِهِ لِخُصُوصِ الْمَادَّةِ؛ نَحْوُ: «الْوَاحِدُ رُبُعُ الاثْنَيْنِ»، وَخَبَرُ مُسَيْلِمَةَ بِأَنَّهُ نَبِيٌّ، وَالدَّجَّالُ بِأَنَّهُ إِلَهُ، فَإِنَّهُ مَقْطُوعٌ بِكَذِبِهِ لِخُصُوصِ الْمَادَّةِ لِقِيَامِ الْبُرْهَانِ الْقَاطِعِ عَلَى كَذِبِهِ، لَا لِذَاتِ الْخَبَرِ، وَلَا لِخُصُوصِ الْمُخْبِرِ بِالْكَسْرِ - كَمَا قِيلَ.
The constructive and incomplete relation admit truth and falsity, and that is outside their two concepts, and therefore he added in the definition his statement: 'by itself'. And the definition includes: the compound explicitly like 'Zaid stood', and implicitly like 'I stand', for its hidden pronoun is in the status of the mentioned, and the whole proposition may be estimated like that occurring after 'na'am' and 'bala'. And the definition also includes: the report that is known to be true due to something external to its reality, by consideration of the informant, like the reports of Allah Most High and the reports of His messengers (peace be upon them) because lying is impossible for them, or by consideration of the specific matter, like 'the one is half of two'. And likewise what is known to be false due to something external to its reality due to the specific matter, like 'the one is a quarter of two', and the report of Musaylima that he is a prophet, and the Dajjal that he is a god, for it is known to be false due to the specific matter because of the conclusive proof of its falsehood, not due to the essence of the report, nor due to the specific informant (with kasr) as has been said.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) by its essential property: the possibility of truth and falsity. The commentator begins by explaining that the constructive (insha'iyya) and incomplete (naqisa) relations—referring to the relation between subject and predicate in a proposition—can be true or false, but that capacity is not part of their conceptual meanings; that is why the definition includes the phrase 'by itself' (li-dhatihi). The definition covers both explicit compound propositions like 'Zaid stood' and implicit ones like the verb 'I stand', whose hidden pronoun functions like an explicit subject. It also covers cases where the entire proposition is implied, such as after the particles 'na'am' and 'bala' in response to a question. Crucially, the definition includes statements that are known to be true or false due to external factors: for example, reports from Allah and His messengers are known to be true because lying is impossible for them, but they are still propositions because they could be false in themselves—it is the impossibility of lying that guarantees truth, not the essence of the report. Similarly, statements like 'the one is half of two' are known true by their matter, while false statements like 'the one is a quarter of two' or the claims of Musaylima and the Dajjal are known false by conclusive proof. The commentator emphasizes that these are false due to the specific matter (khusus al-madda) and the external proof, not because the report itself is incapable of truth. This refines the definition: a proposition remains a proposition even when its truth value is known, because the capacity for the opposite is still present in its essence.

Key Terms
قضية (qadiyya): A composite statement that inherently admits truth and falsity, regardless of actual truth value.
خبر (khabar): Synonym for proposition when viewed as conveying information; it admits truth and falsity.
احتمال الصدق والكذب (ihtimal al-sidq wa al-kadhib): The essential capacity to be true or false, the defining feature of a proposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition, which of the following is NOT an example of a proposition (qadiyya)?
A) The report of Musaylima that he is a prophet.
B) The command 'Stand up!' (qum).
C) The statement 'The one is half of two.'
D) Allah's report that the flood occurred in Noah's time.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is defined by its essential capacity for truth and falsity, not by whether we actually know it to be true or false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the following is a proposition according to the definition: a) Allah's command 'Establish prayer' (in the Quran), b) The statement 'The world is temporally originated' (a kalam claim), c) The question 'Is the Quran created?' Explain your reasoning.
?
Given the hadith 'Actions are by intentions', known to be true from the Prophet, does it cease to be a proposition because it is certainly true? Justify using the concept of li-dhatihi.

Propositions
intermediate
8 min
The Four Types of Propositions and Refutation of a Fifth
Commentator enumerates four types of propositions and refutes al-Yusi's addition of a fifth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any report (khabar) into one of four categories based on its truth-value determination, and refute the claim that a fifth category exists based solely on the informant.

Core Idea
Truth-value is determined by the informant's infallibility, the matter itself, or is left open; a fifth type based solely on the informant is invalid.

In Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh, reports are evaluated for truth and falsehood. The commentator presents a clear four-fold classification: reports that admit both truth and falsity absolutely, reports that are necessarily true due to the informant (e.g., divine revelation) or due to their matter (e.g., necessary truths), and reports that are necessarily false due to their matter. He refutes al-Yusi's proposal of a fifth type—false due to the informant alone—on the grounds that no revealed text declares every utterance of a known liar false; falsehood is determined by the specific content, not the person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline: reports admitting truth and falsity absolutely
Type 1 includes ordinary reports from non-infallible persons, e.g., 'Zaid is standing'. These can be true or false depending on the facts.
2
Recognize reports necessarily true due to the informant
Type 2: reports from an infallible source (e.g., Allah, prophets) are true in themselves, even though the report admits truth/falsity by its nature. Their truth is known externally by the infallibility of the informant.
Example: 'The Quran is the word of Allah' from a prophet.
3
Identify reports necessarily true due to their matter
Type 3: reports whose content is self-evidently true, such as 'One is half of two' or 'Four is even'. Their truth is known from the matter itself.
These are a priori truths.
4
Recognize reports necessarily false due to their matter
Type 4: reports whose content contradicts established truths, such as 'Musaylima is a prophet'. Their falsehood is known from the matter itself, not from the informant.
The commentator insists no report is false solely because of the informant; falsehood is always due to content.
5
Refute al-Yusi's proposed fifth type
Al-Yusi suggested a fifth type: reports false due to the informant alone (e.g., any report from Musaylima). The commentator argues this is invalid because no scripture states everything Musaylima said is false. His specific false claims (e.g., prophethood) are false by the matter, not the person.
The principle: judgments about truth/falsity follow the content, not the person's reputation.

Source Text

قوله: (كَالْوَاقِعَةِ بَعْدَ نَعَمْ، وَبَلَى) أي: الَّتِي يَدُلُّ عَلَيْهَا كَلَامُ السَّائِلِ، وَهَذَا بِنَاءً عَلَى مَا لِلْمُحَقِّقِينَ مِنْ أَنَّ «نَعَمْ» وَ«بَلَى» لَيْسَتَا قَضِيَّتَيْنِ. وَذَهَبَ ابْنُ طَلْحَةَ وَابْنُ عُصْفُورٍ مِنَ النَّحْوِيِّينَ إِلَى أَنَّ «نَعَمْ» وَ«بَلَى» قَضِيَّتَانِ؛ بِنَاءً مِنْهُمْ عَلَى عَدَمِ اشْتِرَاطِ التَّرْكِيبِ فِي الْكَلَامِ. قوله: (كَمَا قِيلَ) أَشَارَ بِهِ لِلرَّدِّ عَلَى الشَّيْخِ الْيُوسِيِّ فِي حَوَاشِي الْمُخْتَصَرِ، فَإِنَّهُ جَعَلَ الْخَبَرَ مِنْ مُسَيْلِمَةَ مِمَّا قُطِعَ بِكَذِبِهِ لِلْمُخْبِرِ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرُ كَلَامِ قَدُّورَةَ أَيْضاً، وَلَيْسَ الْأَمْرُ كَذَلِكَ، فَأَقْسَامُ الْخَبَرِ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَ الشَّارِحُ أَرْبَعَةٌ فَقَطْ، لَا خَمْسَةً خِلَافاً لِلْيُوسِيِّ: 1 - مَا يَحْتَمِلُ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ مُطْلَقاً؛ كَخَبَرِ مَنْ لَيْسَ مَعْصُوماً بِنَحْوِ: «قِيَامُ زَيْدٍ». 2 - وَمَا يَحْتَمِلُهُمَا لِذَاتِهِ وَتَعَيَّنَ صِدْقُهُ نَظَراً لِلْخَارِجِ مِنْ مُخْبِرٍ كَخَبَرِ مَنْ يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ الْكَذِبُ. 3 - أَوْ نَظَراً لِخُصُوصِ الْمَادَّةِ؛ نَحْوُ: الْوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَيْنِ»، وَ: «الْأَرْبَعَةُ زَوْجٌ». 4 - أَوْ تَعَيَّنَ كِذْبُهُ نَظَراً إِلَى الْمُخْبِرِ بِهِ. وَلَيْسَ عِنْدَنَا مَا هُوَ مَقْطُوعٌ بِكَذِبِهِ نَظَراً إِلَى الْمُخْبِرِ؛ إِذْ لَمْ يَرِدْ نَصٌّ مِنَ الشَّرْعِ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا أَخْبَرَ بِهِ مُسَيْلِمَةُ أَوِ الدَّجَّالُ كَذِبٌ، وَخَبَرُ دَعْوَى مُسَيْلِمَةَ الرِّسَالَةَ وَالدَّجَّالِ الْأُلُوهِيَّةَ مَقْطُوعٌ بِكَذِبِهِ لِخُصُوصِ الْمَادَّةِ. قوله: (الْقَضِيَّةَ الْعَقْلِيَّةَ) أَيِ الْمَفْهُومَ الْعَقْلِيَّ الْمُرَكَّبَ مِنَ: الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ، وَبِهِ، وَالْحُكْمِ؛ الْمُسَمَّاةَ بِـ «الْقَضِيَّةِ» حَقِيقَةً، وَإِطْلَاقُهَا عَلَى الْمَلْفُوظَةِ مَجَازٌ؛ لِدَلَالَتِهَا عَلَيْهَا، كَمَا اخْتَارَهُ السَّيِّدُ. وَقِيلَ: إِنَّ إِطْلَاقَ الْقَضِيَّةِ عَلَى الْعَقْلِيَّةِ وَاللَّفْظِيَّةِ بِالِاشْتِرَاكِ، لَا بِالْحَقِيقَةِ وَالْمَجَازِ. قوله: (إِلَّا فِي التَّرَدُّدِ بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ ... إلخ) فَلَا يُقَالُ لِمَا لَيْسَ لَهُ إِلَّا مَعْنَى وَاحِدٌ هُوَ مَحْتَمِلٌ لَهُ، وَالْمُعَيَّنُ لِلْمَحْذُوفِ الْعِلْمُ فَإِنَّ مُقَابِلَ الصِّدْقِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الْكَذِبُ. قوله: (أَيِ الْحُكْمِ) أَيْ: وَعَلَيْهِ فَتَسْمِيَتُهَا بِذَلِكَ مِنْ تَسْمِيَةِ الشَّيْءِ بِاسْمِ جُزْئِهِ، فَـ«الْقَضِيَّةُ»: «فَعِيلَةٌ» مِنَ الْقَضَاءِ؛ أَيِ الْحُكْمِ؛ بِمَعْنَى: «مَفْعُولَةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا مَقْضِيٌّ فِيهَا بِثُبُوتِ النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا. قوله: (وَيُسَمَّى: خَبَراً ... إلخ) قَالَ السَّيِّدُ فِي «التَّلْوِيحِ: اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُرَكَّبَ التَّامَّ الْمُحْتَمِلَ لِلصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ يُسَمَّى: - مِنْ حَيْثُ اشْتِمَالُهُ عَلَى الْحُكْمِ: «قَضِيَّةٌ». - وَمِنْ حَيْثُ احْتِمَالُهُ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ: «خَبَراً». - وَمِنْ حَيْثُ إِفَادَتُهُ الْحُكْمَ: «إِخْبَاراً». - وَمِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنُهُ جُزْءاً مِنَ الدَّلِيلِ: «مُقَدِّمَةً».
His statement: 'like that occurring after na'am and bala' means that which the speech of the questioner indicates, and this is based on what the verifiers hold that 'na'am' and 'bala' are not two propositions. Ibn Talha and Ibn 'Asfur among the grammarians held that 'na'am' and 'bala' are two propositions, based on their not stipulating composition in speech. His statement: 'as was said' he indicated by it the refutation of al-Shaykh al-Yusi in his marginal notes on al-Mukhtasar, for he made the report from Musaylima as that which is known to be false due to the informant, and that is also apparent from the speech of Qaddura, and it is not so. For the divisions of the report according to what the commentator mentioned are only four, not five, contrary to al-Yusi: 1 - What admits truth and falsity absolutely, like the report of one who is not infallible, such as 'the standing of Zaid'. 2 - And what admits them in itself and its truth is determined by consideration of the external from the informant, like the report of one for whom lying is impossible. 3 - Or by consideration of the specific matter, like 'the one is half of two' and 'four is even'. 4 - Or its falsehood is determined by consideration of the informed about (al-mukhbar bihi). And we do not have anything that is known to be false by consideration of the informant, since there is no revealed text that everything that Musaylima or the Dajjal reported is false, and the report of Musaylima's claim of prophethood and the Dajjal's claim of divinity is known to be false due to the specific matter. His statement: 'the mental proposition' means the mental concept composed of the subject, predicate, and judgment, which is called 'proposition' in reality, and its application to the uttered one is metaphorical, because it indicates it, as al-Sayyid chose. And it was said that the application of 'proposition' to the mental and the uttered is by homonymy, not by reality and metaphor. His statement: 'except in the hesitation between two... etc.' So it is not said of what has only one meaning that it is a possibility of it, and the determiner of the omitted is knowledge, for the opposite of truth is only falsehood. His statement: 'i.e. the judgment' means accordingly that naming it that is naming the thing by the name of its part, for 'al-qadiyya' is a fa'ila form from al-qada' meaning judgment, in the sense of maf'ula, because it is that in which the affirmation of the relation between its two extremes is judged. His statement: 'and it is called khabar... etc.' al-Sayyid said in al-Talwih: Know that the complete composite that admits truth and falsehood is called: - from the aspect of its inclusion of judgment: 'qadiyya'. - And from the aspect of its admission of truth and falsehood: 'khabar'. - And from the aspect of its conveying the judgment: 'ikhbar'. - And from the aspect of its being a part of the proof: 'muqaddima'.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage begins with a super-commentary clarifying the status of the particles 'na'am' and 'bala' as potential propositions. The commentator mentions that according to the verifiers, these particles are not themselves complete propositions, but some grammarians argue otherwise if composition is not required. The main point of the lesson is a classification of propositions into four types, refuting al-Yusi who proposed a fifth type based on the informant. The four types are: (1) statements that admit truth and falsity without restriction, like ordinary reports from non-infallible persons; (2) statements that admit truth and falsity in themselves but are known to be true due to the infallibility of the informant (e.g., divine reports); (3) statements known true due to their specific matter (e.g., necessary truths like 'the one is half of two'); and (4) statements known false due to the matter informed about (e.g., Musaylima's claim of prophethood). The commentator stresses that there is no type known false solely due to the informant, because no text declares that every utterance of Musaylima is false—only his specific false claims are false by the matter. The passage then delves into the terminology: 'qadiyya' (proposition) in its primary sense refers to the mental judgment composed of subject, predicate, and judgment, while its application to uttered expressions is metaphorical. Alternative views consider it homonymous. The synonymy is clarified: 'qadiyya' emphasizes judgment, 'khabar' emphasizes the possibility of truth/falsity, 'ikhbar' emphasizes the communicative act, and 'muqaddima' emphasizes its role as a premise in reasoning. This terminological precision helps students understand the different facets of a proposition across logical contexts.

Key Terms
قضية
خبر
صدق
كذب
المخبر به

Key Takeaway
A proposition's truth or falsehood is determined either by the informant's infallibility, the matter itself, or is open; a fifth type based solely on the informant is invalid because falsehood always relates to the content, not the person.

Propositions

The Terminology of 'Proposition' (Qadiyya) and Its Synonyms
Commentator clarifies terminological distinctions for 'qadiyya' and its synonyms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the terms 'qadiyya', 'khabar', 'ikhbar', and 'muqaddima' based on their specific logical aspects, and identify the primary meaning of 'qadiyya' as a mental judgment.

Core Idea
'Qadiyya' primarily means a mental judgment; its use for uttered sentences is metaphorical, and it is called 'khabar' for truth/falsity, 'ikhbar' for informing, and 'muqaddima' as a premise.

In Islamic logic, precise terminology is essential. The same composite statement can be referred to by different names depending on the aspect considered: 'qadiyya' highlights the judgment itself, 'khabar' emphasizes the possibility of truth or falsehood, 'ikhbar' focuses on the act of informing, and 'muqaddima' points to its role in a proof. Understanding these nuances prevents confusion in logical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary meaning of 'qadiyya'
The term 'qadiyya' (فَعِيلَة from قضاء) originally refers to a judgment (hukm). It is applied to the mental composition of subject, predicate, and judgment. This is the real (haqiqi) meaning.
Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani preferred this view.
2
Distinguish the metaphorical usage for uttered propositions
When 'qadiyya' is used for spoken sentences, it is metaphorical because they indicate the mental judgment. Some scholars consider it homonymous (ishtirak) rather than metaphorical.
3
Recognize the synonym 'khabar'
The same composite is called 'khabar' when considering its property of admitting truth and falsehood. This aspect is crucial in hadith criticism.
4
Understand 'ikhbar' and 'muqaddima'
'Ikhbar' refers to the act of informing, emphasizing the communicative function. 'Muqaddima' is used when the proposition serves as a premise in an argument, highlighting its logical role.
These terms are not mutually exclusive; they describe the same entity from different perspectives.

قوله: (كَالْوَاقِعَةِ بَعْدَ نَعَمْ، وَبَلَى) أي: الَّتِي يَدُلُّ عَلَيْهَا كَلَامُ السَّائِلِ، وَهَذَا بِنَاءً عَلَى مَا لِلْمُحَقِّقِينَ مِنْ أَنَّ «نَعَمْ» وَ«بَلَى» لَيْسَتَا قَضِيَّتَيْنِ. وَذَهَبَ ابْنُ طَلْحَةَ وَابْنُ عُصْفُورٍ مِنَ النَّحْوِيِّينَ إِلَى أَنَّ «نَعَمْ» وَ«بَلَى» قَضِيَّتَانِ؛ بِنَاءً مِنْهُمْ عَلَى عَدَمِ اشْتِرَاطِ التَّرْكِيبِ فِي الْكَلَامِ. قوله: (كَمَا قِيلَ) أَشَارَ بِهِ لِلرَّدِّ عَلَى الشَّيْخِ الْيُوسِيِّ فِي حَوَاشِي الْمُخْتَصَرِ، فَإِنَّهُ جَعَلَ الْخَبَرَ مِنْ مُسَيْلِمَةَ مِمَّا قُطِعَ بِكَذِبِهِ لِلْمُخْبِرِ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرُ كَلَامِ قَدُّورَةَ أَيْضاً، وَلَيْسَ الْأَمْرُ كَذَلِكَ، فَأَقْسَامُ الْخَبَرِ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَ الشَّارِحُ أَرْبَعَةٌ فَقَطْ، لَا خَمْسَةً خِلَافاً لِلْيُوسِيِّ: 1 - مَا يَحْتَمِلُ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ مُطْلَقاً؛ كَخَبَرِ مَنْ لَيْسَ مَعْصُوماً بِنَحْوِ: «قِيَامُ زَيْدٍ». 2 - وَمَا يَحْتَمِلُهُمَا لِذَاتِهِ وَتَعَيَّنَ صِدْقُهُ نَظَراً لِلْخَارِجِ مِنْ مُخْبِرٍ كَخَبَرِ مَنْ يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ الْكَذِبُ. 3 - أَوْ نَظَراً لِخُصُوصِ الْمَادَّةِ؛ نَحْوُ: الْوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَيْنِ»، وَ: «الْأَرْبَعَةُ زَوْجٌ». 4 - أَوْ تَعَيَّنَ كِذْبُهُ نَظَراً إِلَى الْمُخْبِرِ بِهِ. وَلَيْسَ عِنْدَنَا مَا هُوَ مَقْطُوعٌ بِكَذِبِهِ نَظَراً إِلَى الْمُخْبِرِ؛ إِذْ لَمْ يَرِدْ نَصٌّ مِنَ الشَّرْعِ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا أَخْبَرَ بِهِ مُسَيْلِمَةُ أَوِ الدَّجَّالُ كَذِبٌ، وَخَبَرُ دَعْوَى مُسَيْلِمَةَ الرِّسَالَةَ وَالدَّجَّالِ الْأُلُوهِيَّةَ مَقْطُوعٌ بِكَذِبِهِ لِخُصُوصِ الْمَادَّةِ. قوله: (الْقَضِيَّةَ الْعَقْلِيَّةَ) أَيِ الْمَفْهُومَ الْعَقْلِيَّ الْمُرَكَّبَ مِنَ: الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ، وَبِهِ، وَالْحُكْمِ؛ الْمُسَمَّاةَ بِـ «الْقَضِيَّةِ» حَقِيقَةً، وَإِطْلَاقُهَا عَلَى الْمَلْفُوظَةِ مَجَازٌ؛ لِدَلَالَتِهَا عَلَيْهَا، كَمَا اخْتَارَهُ السَّيِّدُ. وَقِيلَ: إِنَّ إِطْلَاقَ الْقَضِيَّةِ عَلَى الْعَقْلِيَّةِ وَاللَّفْظِيَّةِ بِالِاشْتِرَاكِ، لَا بِالْحَقِيقَةِ وَالْمَجَازِ. قوله: (إِلَّا فِي التَّرَدُّدِ بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ ... إلخ) فَلَا يُقَالُ لِمَا لَيْسَ لَهُ إِلَّا مَعْنَى وَاحِدٌ هُوَ مَحْتَمِلٌ لَهُ، وَالْمُعَيَّنُ لِلْمَحْذُوفِ الْعِلْمُ فَإِنَّ مُقَابِلَ الصِّدْقِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الْكَذِبُ. قوله: (أَيِ الْحُكْمِ) أَيْ: وَعَلَيْهِ فَتَسْمِيَتُهَا بِذَلِكَ مِنْ تَسْمِيَةِ الشَّيْءِ بِاسْمِ جُزْئِهِ، فَـ«الْقَضِيَّةُ»: «فَعِيلَةٌ» مِنَ الْقَضَاءِ؛ أَيِ الْحُكْمِ؛ بِمَعْنَى: «مَفْعُولَةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا مَقْضِيٌّ فِيهَا بِثُبُوتِ النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا. قوله: (وَيُسَمَّى: خَبَراً ... إلخ) قَالَ السَّيِّدُ فِي «التَّلْوِيحِ: اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُرَكَّبَ التَّامَّ الْمُحْتَمِلَ لِلصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ يُسَمَّى: - مِنْ حَيْثُ اشْتِمَالُهُ عَلَى الْحُكْمِ: «قَضِيَّةٌ». - وَمِنْ حَيْثُ احْتِمَالُهُ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ: «خَبَراً». - وَمِنْ حَيْثُ إِفَادَتُهُ الْحُكْمَ: «إِخْبَاراً». - وَمِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنُهُ جُزْءاً مِنَ الدَّلِيلِ: «مُقَدِّمَةً».
His statement: 'like that occurring after na'am and bala' means that which the speech of the questioner indicates, and this is based on what the verifiers hold that 'na'am' and 'bala' are not two propositions. Ibn Talha and Ibn 'Asfur among the grammarians held that 'na'am' and 'bala' are two propositions, based on their not stipulating composition in speech. His statement: 'as was said' he indicated by it the refutation of al-Shaykh al-Yusi in his marginal notes on al-Mukhtasar, for he made the report from Musaylima as that which is known to be false due to the informant, and that is also apparent from the speech of Qaddura, and it is not so. For the divisions of the report according to what the commentator mentioned are only four, not five, contrary to al-Yusi: 1 - What admits truth and falsity absolutely, like the report of one who is not infallible, such as 'the standing of Zaid'. 2 - And what admits them in itself and its truth is determined by consideration of the external from the informant, like the report of one for whom lying is impossible. 3 - Or by consideration of the specific matter, like 'the one is half of two' and 'four is even'. 4 - Or its falsehood is determined by consideration of the informed about (al-mukhbar bihi). And we do not have anything that is known to be false by consideration of the informant, since there is no revealed text that everything that Musaylima or the Dajjal reported is false, and the report of Musaylima's claim of prophethood and the Dajjal's claim of divinity is known to be false due to the specific matter. His statement: 'the mental proposition' means the mental concept composed of the subject, predicate, and judgment, which is called 'proposition' in reality, and its application to the uttered one is metaphorical, because it indicates it, as al-Sayyid chose. And it was said that the application of 'proposition' to the mental and the uttered is by homonymy, not by reality and metaphor. His statement: 'except in the hesitation between two... etc.' So it is not said of what has only one meaning that it is a possibility of it, and the determiner of the omitted is knowledge, for the opposite of truth is only falsehood. His statement: 'i.e. the judgment' means accordingly that naming it that is naming the thing by the name of its part, for 'al-qadiyya' is a fa'ila form from al-qada' meaning judgment, in the sense of maf'ula, because it is that in which the affirmation of the relation between its two extremes is judged. His statement: 'and it is called khabar... etc.' al-Sayyid said in al-Talwih: Know that the complete composite that admits truth and falsehood is called: - from the aspect of its inclusion of judgment: 'qadiyya'. - And from the aspect of its admission of truth and falsehood: 'khabar'. - And from the aspect of its conveying the judgment: 'ikhbar'. - And from the aspect of its being a part of the proof: 'muqaddima'.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage begins with a super-commentary clarifying the status of the particles 'na'am' and 'bala' as potential propositions. The commentator mentions that according to the verifiers, these particles are not themselves complete propositions, but some grammarians argue otherwise if composition is not required. The main point of the lesson is a classification of propositions into four types, refuting al-Yusi who proposed a fifth type based on the informant. The four types are: (1) statements that admit truth and falsity without restriction, like ordinary reports from non-infallible persons; (2) statements that admit truth and falsity in themselves but are known to be true due to the infallibility of the informant (e.g., divine reports); (3) statements known true due to their specific matter (e.g., necessary truths like 'the one is half of two'); and (4) statements known false due to the matter informed about (e.g., Musaylima's claim of prophethood). The commentator stresses that there is no type known false solely due to the informant, because no text declares that every utterance of Musaylima is false—only his specific false claims are false by the matter. The passage then delves into the terminology: 'qadiyya' (proposition) in its primary sense refers to the mental judgment composed of subject, predicate, and judgment, while its application to uttered expressions is metaphorical. Alternative views consider it homonymous. The synonymy is clarified: 'qadiyya' emphasizes judgment, 'khabar' emphasizes the possibility of truth/falsity, 'ikhbar' emphasizes the communicative act, and 'muqaddima' emphasizes its role as a premise in reasoning. This terminological precision helps students understand the different facets of a proposition across logical contexts.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is called 'qadiyya' for its judgment, 'khabar' for its truth/falsity, 'ikhbar' for informing, and 'muqaddima' as a premise; each name focuses on a distinct function in logic.

Definitions of Truth and Falsehood Three Schools on Correspondence
Author defines truth and falsehood and presents the positions of Ahl al-Sunna, al-Nazzam, and al-Jahiz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the definitions of truth and falsehood according to Ahl al-Sunna, al-Nazzam, and al-Jahiz, and classify the six cases of khabar accordingly.

Core Idea
Truth is not simply correspondence to belief — three schools disagree on what truth depends on.

In this passage, the author defines truth and falsehood in terms of correspondence between a statement (khabar) and reality. He then presents the positions of the Sunni mainstream, al-Nazzam, and al-Jahiz, each of whom defines truth relative to belief and/or reality in different ways. Understanding these definitions is essential for evaluating statements in theology and logic, especially when belief and reality diverge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions of truth
Find the definitions given for truth: Ahl al-Sunna: correspondence with reality; al-Nazzam: correspondence with belief only; al-Jahiz: correspondence with both reality and belief.
The definition of falsehood is simply the opposite of truth in each view.
2
Recognize the six possible relations between khabar waqi and i'tiqad
The commentator explains that 'correspondence with reality' can occur whether belief agrees, disagrees, or is absent. Thus six cases emerge: (1) matches reality and belief, (2) matches reality but contradicts belief, (3) matches reality with no belief, (4) contradicts reality but matches belief, (5) contradicts reality and belief, (6) contradicts reality with no belief.
These six are exhaustive for any statement considered.
3
Apply each schools definition to the six cases
For each case, determine if it is truth, falsehood, or neither according to each school. For example, case (2): matches reality but contradicts belief. Ahl al-Sunna: truth; al-Nazzam: falsehood; al-Jahiz: neither.
4
Summarize the agreed and disputed cases
Cases (1) and (5) are agreed: (1) is truth for all, (5) is falsehood for all. The other four cases are disputed among the three schools. Note how al-Jahiz introduces a middle category.
Understanding this taxonomy is key to evaluating statements in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: الصِّدْقُ: «مُطَابَقَةُ الخَبَرِ لِلْوَاقِعِ وفَاقاً لِأَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ، لَا لِلاعْتِقَادِ فَقَدْ، وَلَا لِلاعْتِقَادِ مَعَ الوَاقِعِ خِلَافاً لِغَيْرِهِمْ، وَالكَذِبُ خِلافُهُ، وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «الخَبَرِ» فِي تَعْرِيفِهِمَا مَعْنَاهُ اللُّغَوِيُّ، قوله : (لِلْوَاقِعِ) أي : طابق الاعتقاد أو خالفه، أو لم يصحبه اعتقاد، والكذب ما خالف الواقع كذلك، فهذه أقسام ستَّةٌ . قوله : (لِلاعْتِقَادِ فَقَط) أي : كما هو مذهب النَّظَّام. قوله : (وَلَا لِلاعْتِقَادِ مَعَ الوَاقِعِ) أي : كما هو مذهب الجاحظ، فهو يثبت الواسطة، فالصدق مطابقة الخبر لهما، والكذب مخالفته لهما ،وغيرهما، وهو أربعة أقسام ليس بصدق ولا كذب، وعلى هذه الأقوال الثلاثة اقتصر صاحب التلخيص»، فالمطابق للواقع والاعتقاد صادق اتفاقاً، والمخالف لهما كاذب اتّفاقاً والمطابق للواقع المخالف للاعتقاد الجمهورُ صادقٌ، والنَّظَّام كاذب، والجاحظ غيرهما ; أي : غير الصدق والكذب . وعكسه الجمهور كاذب، والنظام صادق، والجاحظ لا ولا . والمطابق للواقع الذي لا اعتقاد فيه : الجمهور صادق، والنَّظَّام كاذب، والجاحظ لا ولا . والمخالف للواقع الذي لا اعتقاد فيه : الجمهور كاذب ، والنَّظامُ كذلك، والجاحظ لا ولا .
Notice: Truth is the correspondence of the khabar with reality, in agreement with Ahl al-Sunna, not with belief only, nor with belief together with reality, contrary to others. Falsehood is its opposite. What is intended by 'khabar' in their definition is its linguistic meaning. He said: (with reality), i.e., whether it corresponds to belief or contradicts it, or is not accompanied by belief. Falsehood is what contradicts reality likewise. So these are six divisions. He said: (with belief only), i.e., as is the doctrine of al-Nazzam. He said: (nor with belief together with reality), i.e., as is the doctrine of al-Jahiz, for he affirms an intermediate position. Thus truth is the correspondence of the khabar to both, falsehood is its contradiction to both, and other than those two are four divisions that are neither truth nor falsehood. On these three doctrines the author of al-Talkhis limited himself. So what corresponds to reality and belief is truth by agreement. What contradicts both is falsehood by agreement. What corresponds to reality but contradicts belief: the majority (Ahl al-Sunna) consider it truth, al-Nazzam considers it falsehood, and al-Jahiz considers it something else (neither truth nor falsehood). Its opposite (i.e., contradicts reality but corresponds to belief): the majority consider it falsehood, al-Nazzam considers it truth, and al-Jahiz considers it neither. What corresponds to reality with no belief: the majority consider it truth, al-Nazzam considers it falsehood, and al-Jahiz considers it neither. What contradicts reality with no belief: the majority consider it falsehood, and similarly al-Nazzam, and al-Jahiz considers it neither.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage is a notice (tanbih) that defines truth and falsehood according to the logicians. It begins by stating the definition of truth as 'correspondence of the khabar with reality' (al-waqi'), which is the position of Ahl al-Sunna (the mainstream Sunni theologians). This definition excludes two other definitions: 'correspondence with belief only' (al-Nazzam's view) and 'correspondence with belief together with reality' (al-Jahiz's view). Falsehood is simply the opposite of truth. The word 'khabar' in these definitions is taken in its linguistic sense (i.e., any statement that can be true or false). The commentator then explains that 'correspondence with reality' means the khabar may agree with belief, contradict it, or not be accompanied by belief at all. Thus there are six possible cases: (1) matches reality and belief, (2) matches reality but contradicts belief, (3) matches reality with no belief, (4) contradicts reality but matches belief, (5) contradicts reality and belief, (6) contradicts reality with no belief. The three schools differ on how to classify these. Al-Nazzam defines truth as correspondence with belief only, so for him case (1) is truth, case (2) is falsehood (because it contradicts belief), case (3) is falsehood (because no belief, so not corresponding to belief), case (4) is truth (matches belief despite contradicting reality), etc. Al-Jahiz adds a third value: he says truth requires correspondence to both reality and belief, falsehood requires contradiction to both, and the other four cases are neither truth nor falsehood (a middle state). The author of al-Talkhis (likely al-Taftazani) mentions only these three views. The passage then summarizes the verdict of each school on the six cases: cases (1) and (5) are agreed upon as truth and falsehood respectively; the other four are disputed. For example, a statement that corresponds to reality but contradicts belief is truth according to the majority, falsehood according to al-Nazzam, and neither according to al-Jahiz. A statement that contradicts reality but corresponds to belief is falsehood for the majority, truth for al-Nazzam, and neither for al-Jahiz. A statement that corresponds to reality without any belief is truth for the majority, falsehood for al-Nazzam, and neither for al-Jahiz. A statement that contradicts reality without belief is falsehood for both majority and al-Nazzam, but neither for al-Jahiz. This nuanced classification is important for understanding how different theologians define truth, especially in cases where belief may be absent or contrary to reality.

اعتقاد
مطابقة

B) Falsehood

D) Both truth and falsehood

Key Takeaway
The definition of truth varies among theologians: Ahl al-Sunna define it by correspondence to reality, al-Nazzam by correspondence to belief, and al-Jahiz by correspondence to both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The world is created.' According to Ahl al-Sunna, al-Nazzam, and al-Jahiz, classify the truth-status of this statement under each of the following scenarios: (a) The speaker believes the world is created and it is indeed created. (b) The speaker does not believe the world is created but it is created. (c) The speaker believes the world is created but it is not created. (d) The speaker has no belief about the creation of the world, and the world is created.
?
Identify which of the six cases of khabar corresponds to each scenario above.

Resolving Circularity in Defining Khabar
Addressing circularity objection in defining khabar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circularity (dawr) in a definition and resolve it by distinguishing between linguistic and technical senses of a term.

Core Idea
Circularity in definitions is dissolved by distinguishing a term's linguistic and technical senses.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential logical flaw: defining 'khabar' using truth and falsehood, while truth and falsehood are themselves defined using 'khabar'. This seems circular. However, the resolution lies in noting that 'khabar' is used in two different senses. We will explore this objection and its solution, a key tool in Islamic logic for avoiding circular definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions involved
Truth is defined as conformity of khabar to reality; falsehood as non-conformity. Khabar is defined using truth and falsehood. Notice the reciprocal dependency.
2
Recognize the circularity
If 'khabar' is taken in the same sense in both definitions, each presupposes the other. This is the logical fallacy of dawr.
3
Distinguish the two senses
The answer: 'khabar' in the definitions of truth/falsehood means the act of informing (linguistic sense), while the 'khabar' being defined is the proposition (technical sense). No circle.
4
Examine alternative answers
The passage also notes two other resolutions: truth/falsehood as attributes of the speaker vs. speech, or that truth/falsehood are so well-known they need no definition.
These are supplementary but the main resolution is the sense distinction.
5
Apply to a new example
Practice by identifying a similar circularity in a different definition (e.g., defining 'wajib' using 'thawab' and 'thawab' using 'wajib').

Source Text

باب في القضايا وأحكامها
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «الخَبَرِ») الَّذِي عَرَّفَ بِهِمَا كَمَا فِي النَّظْمِ مَعْنَاهُ الِاصْطِلَاحِيُّ، فَلَا دَوْرَ؛ تَأَمَّلْ.
وَاخْتُلِفَ فِي مَدْلُولِ الخَبَرِ :
فَقِيلَ : هُوَ حُصُولُ النِّسْبَةِ فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ . وَقِيلَ : هُوَ الحُكْمُ بِالنُّبُوتِ أَوِ الانْتِفَاء .
وَالتَّحْقِيقُ عِنْدَ السَّعْدِ فِي المُطَوَّلِ الأَوَّلُ ، وَعِنْدَ السُّبْكِي الثَّانِي، وَعَلَى الأَوَّلِ
قصارة.
قوله : (وَالمُرَادُ بِالخَبَرِ ... إلخ) هذا جواب عن اعتراض يَرِدُ على الناظم، وهو: أنَّه أدخل الصدق والكذب في تعريف الخبر، مع أنَّ الصِّدق: «مطابقة الخبر للواقع»، والكذب: «عدم مطابقة الخبر ... إلخ»، فتوقف كلٌّ منهما على الآخر، فيلزم الدور.
والجواب: المراد بـ «الخبر في تعريفهما - أي: الصدق والكذب - معناه اللغوي؛ أي : الإخبار؛ أي: إلقاء الخبر، والخبر الذي عرف بهما ـ أي: الصدق والكذب ـ مـعـنـاه الاصطلاحي ؛ أي: القضيَّة، فلا دور.
وأصلُ هذا الجواب للسّعد ؛ قال : وأيضاً الصدق والكذب المأخوذان في تعريف الخبر وصفان للمتكلّم ؛ أي: الإعلام بالشَّيء على ما هو عليه، أو على خلافه، والمعرفان بالخبر وصفان للكلام. وأجيب أيضاً بأنَّ الصدق والكذب قدِ اشتهر بين الخاصة والعامة حتّى استغنيا عن التعريف، وحينئذٍ فيصحُ تعريف الخبر بهما، ولا دور. قوله : (فَقِيلَ: هُوَ حُصُولُ النِّسْبَةِ أي : الثَّبوت والانتفاء في نفس الأمر، وهو للقرافي
في «شرح المحصول».
قوله : (وَقِيلَ: هُوَ الحُكْمُ بِالنُّبُوتِ) أي: إدراك أنَّ النِّسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة، وهو قول الإمام» في المحصول.
قوله : ( وَالتَّحْقِيقُ عِنْدَ السَّعْدِ فِي المُطَوَّلِ الأَوَّلُ... إلخ) هذا هو الذي ارتضاه ابن أبي شريف في حواشي المحلي»، وهو الظاهر؛ لأنَّا نقطع بأنَّ الَّذي نقصده بقولنا : «زيد قائم هو إفادة المخاطب ثبوت القيام لزيد، لا حكمنا بذلك، ولهذا يصح إذا قيل لك: «من أين تعلم هذا؟»
أن تقول : سمعتُهُ مِن فلان، ولو كان مفهوم القضيَّة هو الحكم لكان واقعاً دائماً .
قوله : (وَعِنْدَ السُّبْكِي الثَّانِي ظاهرُ كلام المحلي ترجيح هذا القول.
Chapter on propositions and their rulings. And what is meant by 'khabar' that was defined by them as in the poem is its technical meaning, so there is no circularity; reflect. And there is disagreement concerning the meaning of khabar: it is said: it is the occurrence of the relation in reality itself. And it is said: it is the judgment of affirmation or negation. And the verified view according to al-Sa'd in al-Mutawwal is the first, and according to al-Subki the second, and upon the first... His saying: 'what is meant by khabar... etc.' This is an answer to an objection that occurs to the author: that he included truth and falsehood in the definition of khabar, while truth is 'conformity of khabar to reality' and falsehood is 'non-conformity of khabar... etc.', so each depends on the other, leading to circularity. The answer: what is meant by 'khabar' in the definition of both – i.e., truth and falsehood – is its linguistic meaning, i.e., informing, i.e., delivering the khabar; and the khabar that is defined by them – i.e., truth and falsehood – its meaning is the technical one, i.e., the proposition, so there is no circularity. The origin of this answer is from al-Sa'd; he said: also, truth and falsehood taken in the definition of khabar are descriptions of the speaker, i.e., informing about something as it is or otherwise, and the definers by khabar are descriptions of speech. And it was also answered that truth and falsehood have become well-known among the elite and general public such that they are independent of definition, and then it is valid to define khabar by them, and there is no circularity. His saying: 'it is said: it is the occurrence of the relation' means: affirmative or negative in reality itself, and this is the view of al-Qarafi in Sharh al-Mahsul. His saying: 'and it is said: it is the judgment of affirmation' means: the perception that the relation exists or does not exist, and this is the saying of al-Imam in al-Mahsul. His saying: 'and the verified view according to al-Sa'd in al-Mutawwal is the first... etc.' This is what Ibn Abi Sharif approved in his glosses on al-Mahalli, and it is the apparent because we are certain that what we intend by our saying 'Zayd is standing' is informing the addressee of the establishment of standing for Zayd, not our judgment of that; and therefore it is valid when it is said to you: 'From where did you learn this?' to say: 'I heard it from so-and-so.' And if the concept of the proposition were the judgment, it would always occur. His saying: 'and according to al-Subki the second' – the apparent meaning of al-Mahalli's words is the preference of this saying.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on propositions (qadaya) and their rulings. It first addresses a potential logical circularity (dawr) in the poem: the poet used truth and falsehood in the definition of khabar (statement), but truth is defined as conformity of khabar to reality, and falsehood as non-conformity. If khabar is taken in the same sense in both, then each would depend on the other. The answer is that 'khabar' in the definitions of truth and falsehood is used in its linguistic sense (al-ikhbar, the act of informing), while the 'khabar' that is being defined (i.e., the subject of truth/falsehood) is used in its technical sense (the proposition itself). Thus no circularity. This answer is attributed to al-Sa'd (i.e., Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani). Alternative answers are also given: truth and falsehood are qualities of the speaker (i.e., truthfulness and falsehood) whereas the defined khabar is a quality of speech, so no circle; or that truth and falsehood are so well-known that they need no definition. Then the passage moves to the disagreement about the meaning (madlul) of khabar: does it signify the occurrence of the relation (nisba) in reality itself (the external relation)? Or does it signify the judgment (hukm) of the speaker affirming or negating? The first opinion is attributed to al-Qarafi, the second to al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). Al-Sa'd in al-Mutawwal prefers the first opinion, while al-Subki prefers the second. The commentator then supports the first opinion as more apparent (al-zahir), arguing that when we say 'Zayd is standing,' we intend to inform the addressee of the fact (thubut al-qiyam), not to express our judgment. If it were the judgment, it would always be present regardless of reality. The example of being asked 'How do you know?' and answering 'I heard it from so-and-so' shows that the proposition conveys information about reality, not just the speaker's judgment. Al-Mahalli's words appear to favor the second opinion according to al-Subki.

خبر
صدق
كذب

Checkpoint
How is the circularity objection in defining khabar using truth and falsehood resolved?
A) By redefining truth as correspondence to belief
B) By using the linguistic sense of khabar in the definition of truth/falsehood and the technical sense in the definition of khabar
C) By denying that truth and falsehood are defined using khabar
D) By positing an intermediate category of neither truth nor falsehood

Key Takeaway
Circular definitions can be resolved by distinguishing linguistic and technical senses of terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition: 'Sahih (valid) is a ruling that is in accordance with the Shari'ah.' But 'Shari'ah' is defined as the rulings themselves. Identify the circularity and propose a resolution by distinguishing linguistic and technical senses.
?
Find another example of circular definition in Islamic sciences (e.g., in defining 'iman' using 'islam' and vice versa) and show how the logicians resolve it.

Propositions
intermediate
8 min
Two Opinions on the Meaning of Khabar
Two opinions on the meaning of khabar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two opinions on the meaning of khabar (the occurrence of the relation vs. the speaker's judgment) and evaluate the evidence for each.

Core Idea
Whether a proposition signifies an external relation or a mental judgment is a foundational dispute in Islamic semantics.

The passage now turns to a disagreement about what exactly a proposition (khabar) signifies. Does it signify the actual occurrence of the relation in reality? Or does it signify the speaker's judgment (hukm) of affirmation or negation? We will examine both views and the arguments for the first being more apparent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Opinion 1: madlul al-khabar = husul al-nisba fi nafs al-amr (the relation's occurrence in reality). Opinion 2: madlul al-khabar = al-hukm bi al-thubut aw al-intifa' (the judgment of affirmation or negation).
2
Note the proponents
Opinion 1 is attributed to al-Qarafi (and preferred by al-Sa'd); opinion 2 to al-Imam (al-Razi) (and preferred by al-Subki).
3
Analyze the commentator's argument
He argues for opinion 1: when we say 'Zayd is standing', our intention is to inform the addressee about the fact, not to express our judgment. Evidence: if asked 'How do you know?', we cite a source, not a judgment.
4
Consider the counterargument
If khabar signified the judgment, then the proposition would always be about the speaker's state, which seems counterintuitive.
5
Evaluate the impact
This distinction affects how we understand truth conditions: truth would then be the correspondence of the judgment to reality versus the correspondence of the statement to reality.

Resolving Circular Definitions: The Case of Khabar
Author defines khabar and addresses the objection of circularity between khabar and truth/falsity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve an apparent circular definition by distinguishing between the linguistic and technical senses of a term.

Core Idea
Circular definitions are avoided when the same word is used in different senses.

When defining terms, we must be careful not to use a term that itself depends on the definition. In this passage, the author faces an objection: khabar is defined using sidq (truth) and kidhb (falsity), but sidq itself is defined using khabar. This seems circular. The commentator shows how to resolve this by distinguishing two meanings of khabar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms involved
Locate the three key terms in the passage: al-khabar, al-sidq, and al-kidhb. Note how each is defined.
2
Examine the definitions
Truth (sidq) is defined as correspondence of khabar to reality; falsity (kidhb) is lack of correspondence. Khabar is defined as a proposition that can be true or false.
The definitions of sidq and kidhb both use 'khabar'.
3
Spot the potential circularity
If 'khabar' in all definitions means the same thing, then khabar depends on sidq/kidhb, and sidq/kidhb depend on khabar, creating a vicious circle.
4
Apply the resolution: distinguish senses
The commentator explains that in the definitions of sidq and kidhb, 'khabar' is used in its linguistic sense (the act of informing). In the definition of khabar itself, it is used in its technical sense (the proposition). Since the senses differ, no circularity occurs.
Also, al-Sa'd adds that sidq/kidhb are attributes of the speaker, while khabar is an attribute of speech.
5
Verify the resolution
Check that the two definitions no longer refer to each other: sidq is correspondence of the informing act to reality; khabar (proposition) is defined using truth/falsity as attributes. The circle is broken.

Source Text

باب في القضايا وأحكامها
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «الخَبَرِ») الَّذِي عَرَّفَ بِهِمَا كَمَا فِي النَّظْمِ مَعْنَاهُ الِاصْطِلَاحِيُّ، فَلَا دَوْرَ؛ تَأَمَّلْ.
قوله : (وَالمُرَادُ بِالخَبَرِ ... إلخ) هذا جواب عن اعتراض يَرِدُ على الناظم، وهو: أنَّه أدخل الصدق والكذب في تعريف الخبر، مع أنَّ الصِّدق: «مطابقة الخبر للواقع»، والكذب: «عدم مطابقة الخبر ... إلخ»، فتوقف كلٌّ منهما على الآخر، فيلزم الدور. والجواب: المراد بـ «الخبر في تعريفهما - أي: الصدق والكذب - معناه اللغوي؛ أي : الإخبار؛ أي: إلقاء الخبر، والخبر الذي عرف بهما ـ أي: الصدق والكذب ـ مـعـنـاه الاصطلاحي ؛ أي: القضيَّة، فلا دور. وأصلُ هذا الجواب للسّعد ; قال : وأيضاً الصدق والكذب المأخوذان في تعريف الخبر وصفان للمتكلّم ; أي: الإعلام بالشَّيء على ما هو عليه، أو على خلافه، والمعرفان بالخبر وصفان للكلام. وأجيب أيضاً بأنَّ الصدق والكذب قدِ اشتهر بين الخاصة والعامة حتّى استغنيا عن التعريف، وحينئذٍ فيصحُ تعريف الخبر بهما، ولا دور.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Judgments. The intended meaning of 'khabar' that is used to define them (i.e., truth and falsity) as in the poem is the technical meaning, so there is no circularity; reflect. His statement (The intended meaning of 'khabar'... etc.) This is a response to an objection that occurs to the author, namely: that he included truth and falsity in the definition of khabar, even though truth is 'the correspondence of khabar to reality' and falsity is 'the non-correspondence of khabar... etc.', so each depends on the other, necessitating circularity. The answer: the intended meaning of 'khabar' in their definition (i.e., truth and falsity) is its linguistic meaning, i.e., informing, i.e., uttering a report, while the khabar that is defined by them (i.e., truth and falsity) is its technical meaning, i.e., the proposition, so there is no circularity. The origin of this answer is from al-Sa'd; he said: also, truth and falsity taken in the definition of khabar are attributes of the speaker, i.e., informing about something as it is or contrary to it, while the two definers are attributes of speech. And it was also answered that truth and falsity are well-known among specialists and the general public such that they are independent of definition, and then it is valid to define khabar by them, and there is no circularity.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on propositions and their judgments. The author first addresses a potential circularity in the definition of 'khabar' (proposition/statement) that appears in the matn. The objection is that truth and falsity are defined in terms of 'khabar', and then 'khabar' is defined using truth and falsity, creating a vicious circle. The commentator resolves this by distinguishing two meanings of 'khabar': in the definitions of truth and falsity, 'khabar' is used in its linguistic sense (the act of informing), while in the definition of 'khabar' as a technical term, it means the proposition itself. Thus, there is no circularity because the term is used in different senses. The commentator also mentions that al-Sa'd added another response: truth and falsity are attributes of the speaker, while the khabar that is defined is an attribute of speech, so again no circularity. A third response is given: truth and falsity are so well-understood that they need no definition, so using them to define 'khabar' is not circular. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that definitions must always avoid using terms that have been defined earlier; here, the usage is not the same.

Key Terms
الخبر
الصدق
الكذب
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is there no circularity in defining 'khabar' using 'sidq' and 'kidhb'?
A) Because the definitions of sidq and kidhb are independent of khabar.
B) Because the khabar in the definition of sidq/kidhb is linguistic, while the khabar defined is technical.
C) Because sidq and kidhb are defined after khabar is defined.
D) Because the matn does not actually use sidq and kidhb in the definition.

Key Takeaway
Circular definitions can be resolved by recognizing that a term may have different meanings in different contexts, such as linguistic vs technical.

If one then defines 'illah as 'that which necessitates a ruling through qiyas', identify the potential circularity and resolve it by distinguishing two senses of qiyas or 'illah.

Classification of Propositions: Categorical vs Conditional
Author divides propositions into conditional and categorical, then subdivides the latter by quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from a conditional proposition based on their composition, and identify the quantifier in a categorical proposition.

Core Idea
Propositions are either categorical (two terms) or conditional (two propositions); categoricals are further classified by quantity.

This lesson unpacks the foundational classification of propositions in Islamic logic. You will learn how to tell whether a statement is a simple subject-predicate proposition or a compound conditional, and then how to read the quantifier that determines its scope. The passage comes from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani's poem on logic.

Guided Path
1
Find the two main branches
In the matn, the author says 'propositions are two divisions: conditional and categorical.' Locate in the Arabic the terms شرطية and حملية.
2
Define categorical proposition
The commentary says: if a proposition is composed of two terms (subject and predicate) or something equivalent to two terms, it is categorical. Understand the three cases of equivalence: predicate as a phrase, subject as a phrase, both as phrases.
E.g., 'Zayd is standing' is categorical; 'Zayd is the one who stands' also counts as two terms.
3
Define conditional proposition
If it is composed of two complete propositions not reducible to a single term, it is conditional. Example: 'If you perform Hajj, then you must be in ihram' – the two parts are complete propositions.
Conditionals are not treated as having a subject-predicate structure.
4
Subdivide categorical by quantity
The matn mentions 'universal' (كلي) and 'individual' (شخصي). A universal applies to every individual of the subject; an individual to a specific named entity (e.g., 'Zayd is standing').
Universal categoricals can be quantified or unquantified.
5
Identify the quantifier
For universal categoricals, they are either quantified (مسور) with a sur like 'all', 'some', 'no', 'some not', or unquantified (مهمل). Practice by finding the quantifier in Arabic examples like 'كل مسكر حرام' (every intoxicant is forbidden).
Unquantified (مهمل) statements lack an explicit quantifier but are often treated as universal in context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ وَالثَّانِي:(٥٢) كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَالأَوَّلُ: إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ، وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ (٥٣) وَالسُّورُ : كُلِّيَّا، وَجُزْئِيًّا يُرَى وَأَرْبَعُ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرَى : (٥٤) إِمَّا بِكُلِّ، أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ، أَوْبِلَا شَيْءٍ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ أَوْ شِبْهِ جَلَا

قوله : (ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ... إلخ): قوله : أَوْ مَا فِي قُوَّتِهِمَا) يشمل ثلاث صور : كون المحمول مفرداً بالقوة، وكون الموضوع مفرداً بالقوة، وكون كل واحدٍ منهما في قوَّة المفرد، وأمثلتهما مذكورة في الشرح.
Then propositions according to them are two divisions: conditional and categorical. And the second: (52) universal and individual. And the first: either quantified or unquantified. (53) The quantifier is seen as universal and particular, and its four divisions wherever it occurs: (54) either 'all', or 'some', or 'nothing', and 'some' or its like is not clear. His statement: (Then propositions according to them are two divisions: conditional and categorical) means: the proposition is divided by a primary division into two divisions: conditional and categorical; because if it is composed of two terms or what is in their power, it is categorical; if it is composed of two propositions not in the interpretation of a single term, then it is conditional. His statement: (Then propositions according to them are two divisions... etc.): his statement 'or what is in their power' includes three cases: the predicate being a single term potentially, the subject being a single term potentially, and each of them being in the power of a single term; and their examples are mentioned in the commentary.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational classification of propositions in logic, as laid out in the matn. The author states that propositions (qadaya) are primarily divided into two: conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya). A categorical proposition is one that predicates something of a subject, composed of two terms (subject and predicate) or something equivalent to them (e.g., a phrase that functions as a single term). A conditional proposition is composed of two complete propositions joined by a conditional particle, such that neither is interpretable as a single term. The passage then further subdivides categorical propositions: they are either universal (kuliyya) or individual (shakhsiyya), and the universal is either quantified (musawwar) with a quantifier like 'all' or 'some', or unquantified (muhmala). The quantifier itself is classified into universal (e.g., 'all') and particular (e.g., 'some'), and the quantifier has four divisions when applied: either 'all' (universal affirmative), 'some' (particular affirmative), 'nothing' (universal negative), and the fourth is 'some not' (particular negative), though the poem says 'and 'some' or its like is not clear' referring to the negative particular. The commentary expands on the definition of categorical propositions by explaining that 'what is in their power' includes three cases: the predicate is a single term in meaning but expressed as a phrase, the subject is similarly, or both. This classification is essential for understanding syllogistic logic, as the type and quantity of propositions determine the validity of syllogisms.

حملية
شرطية
سور

Key Takeaway
The primary division of propositions is into categorical and conditional, determined by whether the components are terms or whole propositions.

5 min
Primary Division of Propositions: Categorical vs Conditional
Author defines the division with composition criterion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the composition criterion to classify any given proposition as categorical or conditional.

Core Idea
A proposition's category depends on whether its parts are terms or complete propositions.

The first and most fundamental division in propositional logic is between categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). The criterion is not about content but about internal composition: if a proposition is made of two terms (or their equivalents), it is categorical; if it is made of two complete propositions, it is conditional. Mastering this classification is essential before studying syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two immediate constituent parts
Every proposition has two main parts: a subject and a predicate (for categorical) or two propositions (for conditional). In 'Zayd is standing,' the parts are 'Zayd' and 'standing'; in 'If Zayd comes, he is honored,' the parts are 'Zayd comes' and 'he is honored'.
2
Check if each part is a term (mufrad) or a proposition (qadiyya)
A term is a single word or a phrase that functions as a single logical unit, like 'Zayd' or 'the father of Zayd'. A proposition is a complete statement that can be true or false. In 'Zayd is standing,' both parts are terms. In 'If Zayd comes, he is honored,' both parts are complete propositions.
3
Apply the classification rule
If both parts are terms (or equivalent to terms), the proposition is categorical (hamliyya). If both parts are complete propositions, it is conditional (shartiyya). This is the primary division; there are no other types at this level.
4
Test with examples from Islamic sciences
Example 1: 'Allah is wise' – parts are 'Allah' and 'wise' (both terms) → categorical. Example 2: 'If the sun rises, it is day' – parts are 'the sun rises' and 'it is day' (both complete propositions) → conditional. Example 3: 'The father of Zayd is generous' – 'the father of Zayd' functions as a term → categorical.
Phrases like 'the father of Zayd' are considered 'what is in the power of a single term' (ma fi quwwatihima).

His statement: 'Then propositions according to them are two kinds: conditional and categorical.' He means: The proposition is primarily divided into two divisions: conditional and categorical. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of propositions. The commentator explains that propositions are primarily divided into two: conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya). The criterion is composition: if the proposition is composed of two single terms (like 'Zayd' and 'wise') or what is equivalent to terms (like phrases that function as a single term), then it is categorical. If it is composed of two complete propositions (like 'If the sun rises, it is day'), then it is conditional. The commentator then elaborates on the phrase 'what is in their power' (ma fi quwwatihima), meaning terms that are not single words but can be expressed as a single word without changing the meaning. Three possibilities are listed: the predicate is potentially a single term, the subject is potentially a single term, or both are. Examples would be provided in the longer commentary (sharh). This classification is fundamental in Islamic logic.
