باب في القضايا وأحكامها
وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يُقْرَنَ بِشَيْءٍ مَعَ صَلَاحِيَّتِهِ لِلسُّوْرِ؛ لِكَوْنِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى الأَفْرَادِ القَابِلَةِ لِلتَّعْمِيمِ وَضِدُّهُ، وَهِيَ: «المُهْمَلَةُ»؛ نَحْوُ: «الحَيَوَانُ إِنْسَانٌ»، «الحَيَوَانُ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ»، سُمِّيَتْ مُهْمَلَةً لِإِهْمَالِهَا مِنَ السُّوْرِ مَعَ صَلَاحِيَّتِهَا لَهُ، أَوْ لِإِهْمَالِ اسْتِعْمَالِهَا فِي الأَدِلَّةِ؛ اسْتِغْنَاءً عَنْهَا بِالجُزْئِيَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِي قُوَّتِهَا.
أَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ المَوْضُوعُ كُلِّيًّا غَيْرَ صَالِحٍ لِلسُّوْرِ؛ لِكَوْنِ الحُكْمِ فِيهَا لَيْسَ عَلَى الأَفْرَادِ القَابِلَةِ لَهُ، بَلْ عَلَى نَفْسِ المَاهِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَا تَعَدُّدَ فِيهَا؛ نَحْوُ: الإِنْسَانُ نَوْعٌ، وَالحَيَوَانُ جِنْسٌ.
قَوْلُهُ: (نَحْوُ: «الحَيَوَانُ إِنْسَانٌ») فَإِنْ قِيلَ: هَذَا المِثَالُ وَنَحْوهُ لَا يَصْلُحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مِثَالًا لِلْمُهْمَلَةِ، وَذَلِكَ أَنَّ المُعَرَّفَ بِـ«أَلْ»: إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهِ المَاهِيَّةُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ مَعَ الحُضُورِ الذِّهْنِيِّ، فَالْقَضِيَّةُ شَخْصِيَّةٌ؛ كَالْمُصَدَّرَةِ بِعَلَمٍ؛ وَإِنْ أُرِيدَ مَعْهُودٌ خَارِجِيُّ، فَهِيَ شَخْصِيَّةٌ أَيْضًا؛ كَالْمُصَدَّرَةِ بِعَلَمِ الشَّخْصِ؛ وَإِنْ أُرِيدَ المَاهِيَّةُ فِي ضِمْنِ جَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ، فَهِيَ كُلِّيَّةٌ؛ كَالْمُسَوَّرَةِ بِـ«كُلِّ»؛ وَإِنْ أُرِيدَ المَاهِيَّةُ فِي ضِمْنِ فَرْدٍ مَّا لِقِيَامِ قَرِينَةٍ، فَجُزْئِيَّةٌ، فَلَا إِهْمَالَ فِي كَلَامِ العَرَبِ. فَالْجَوَابُ: أَنَّ الإِهْمَالَ يَتَحَقَّقُ بِضِمْنِ الحَقِيقَةِ فِي ضِمْنِ الأَفْرَادِ بِلَا اعْتِبَارِ تَبْعِيضٍ فِيهَا، وَلَا تَعْمِيمٍ، بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ وُجُودُهَا لَا فِي ضِمْنِ جَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ، وَلَا فِي ضِمْنِ فَرْدٍ مُعَيَّنٍ، بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنُهَا مُحْتَمِلَةً لِلْكُلِّيَّةِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ، وَهَذَا الوَجْهُ مَذْكُورٌ فِي «المِفْتَاحِ»، فَلَا يُقَالُ: إِنَّهُ وَجْهٌ خَامِسٌ لَمْ يَذْكُرُوهُ. وَأَيْضًا: الِاسْتِغْرَاقُ مُفَوَّضٌ إِلَى المَقَامِ، وَالقَضِيَّةُ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَيْهَا فِي نَفْسِهَا مُحْتَمِلَةٌ لِلْكُلِّيَّةِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ: (سُمِّيَتْ مُهْمَلَةً لِإِهْمَالِهَا) أَيْ: لِأَنَّ الإِهْمَالَ فِي اللُّغَةِ مَعْنَاهُ: «التَّرْكُ»، وَفِي العُرْفِ: تَرْكُ إِدْخَالِ السُّوْرِ فِي القَضِيَّةِ الصَّالِحَةِ لَهُ. قَوْلُهُ: (لِأَنَّهَا فِي قُوَّتِهَا) بَيَانُهُ أَنَّ الحُكْمَ الثَّابِتَ لِلأَفْرَادِ مُطْلَقًا: إِمَّا ثَابِتٌ لِجَمِيعِهَا، أَوْ لِبَعْضِهَا؛ وَأَيًّا مَا كَانَ فَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ صَادِقَةٌ قَطْعًا، وَأَمَّا الكُلِّيَّةُ فَمُحْتَمِلَةٌ، فَجَعَلُوهَا فِي قُوَّةِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ دُونَ الكُلِّيَّةِ؛ أَخْذًا بِالْمُحَقَّقِ، وَطَرْحًا لِلْمَشْكُوكِ.
Chapter on propositions and their rulings. And [the subject] is either not combined with anything while being suitable for the quantifier, because the judgment is on individuals capable of generalization and its opposite, and it is called 'the indefinite' (al-muhmala); as in: 'Animal is human', 'Animal is not a horse'. It was called 'indefinite' due to being left free of the quantifier despite its suitability for it, or due to neglecting its use in arguments, dispensing with it by the particular [proposition] because it is in its potency. As for when the subject is universal but not suitable for the quantifier, because the judgment in it is not on the individuals capable of it, but on the essence itself which has no multiplicity; as in: 'Human is a species', 'Animal is a genus'. His statement: (as in: 'Animal is human') — For it is said: This example and its like are not suitable as an example of the indefinite, because the [noun] defined by 'al': if by it is intended the essence insofar as it is with mental presence, then the proposition is individual (shakhsiyya), like that prefixed with a proper noun; and if intended a specific external [referent], then it is also individual, like that prefixed with a proper noun for a person; and if intended the essence within all individuals, then it is universal (kulliyya), like that quantified with 'every'; and if intended the essence within some individual due to a contextual indicator, then it is particular (juz'iyya). So there is no indefiniteness in the speech of the Arabs. The answer: Indefiniteness is realized by [considering] the reality within individuals without regard to particularization or generalization, but from the perspective of its existence not in all individuals nor in a specific individual, but from the perspective of its being susceptible to universality and particularity. And this aspect is mentioned in 'al-Miftah', so it should not be said that it is a fifth aspect they did not mention. Also: [the consideration of] inclusion (istighraq) is delegated to the context, and the proposition considered in itself is susceptible to universality and particularity. His statement: (It was called 'muhmala' due to its being left free) — i.e., because 'ihmal' in language means 'leaving', and in technical usage: leaving out the quantifier from a proposition suitable for it. His statement: (because it is in its potency) — Its explanation: The judgment established for individuals absolutely: either it is established for all of them or for some of them; and whichever it is, the particular is certainly true, while the universal is uncertain. So they made it in the potency of the particular, not the universal, taking the certain and discarding the doubtful.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage defines the indefinite proposition (muhmala) and addresses a potential objection regarding its example. The author first explains that when a universal subject is suitable for a quantifier but none is used, the proposition is called muhmala. Examples are 'Animal is human' and 'Animal is not a horse'. It is called muhmala either because the quantifier is omitted despite suitability, or because it is neglected in arguments, the particular proposition sufficing since the indefinite is equivalent in force. Next, the author distinguishes this from the 'natural' proposition (tabi'iyya), where the subject is universal but not suitable for a quantifier because the judgment is on the essence, not on individuals (e.g., 'Human is a species'). The commentator then raises an objection: the example 'Animal is human' uses the definite article 'al', which in Arabic has specific meanings (mental presence, external reference, all individuals, or a specific individual) — none of which produce an indefinite proposition. The answer is that the indefinite proposition is not about one of those meanings but about the reality considered in individuals without specifying all or some; it is ambiguous between universal and particular. This view is from 'al-Miftah'. The commentator adds that the potential for inclusion depends on context, and the proposition itself is ambiguous. Finally, the reason the indefinite is considered equivalent to a particular proposition in arguments is explained: the judgment either applies to all or some, and the particular is certainly true in both cases, while the universal is only possible if all are included. Thus, to be certain, they treat the indefinite as particular.

Key Terms
مهملة
سور
جزئية

Why is the indefinite proposition (muhmala) treated as equivalent to a particular proposition in arguments?
A) Because it always refers to a specific individual.
B) Because the judgment is uncertain for all individuals, so assuming it is particular is the safe conclusion.
C) Because the indefinite proposition cannot be used in syllogisms.
D) Because the subject is universal but not suitable for a quantifier.

Key Takeaway
The indefinite proposition (muhmala) is a universal term left unquantified, logically reduced to a particular proposition for certainty in argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the statement: 'The debtor must repay the loan.' This appears in a fiqh manual without a quantifier. Is it muhmala? Apply the definition: the subject 'debtor' is universal and suitable for quantifiers ('every debtor', 'some debtor'). According to the rule, treat it as particular unless context indicates universality. What does this imply for legal reasoning?

Natural Proposition and the Special Meaning of 'Kulliyya'
Author distinguishes natural proposition, explains poet's classification, and lists four quantifier types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a natural proposition from quantified/indefinite propositions and identify when 'kulliyya' means 'having a universal subject' rather than 'universally quantified'.

Core Idea
A natural proposition judges the essence, not individuals; 'kulliyya' can mean subject-universal, not quantified.

In his poem, al-Sullam presents the categorical proposition as 'kulliyya' but then divides it into quantified and indefinite. This seems contradictory if 'kulliyya' meant 'universally quantified'. The commentator al-Bajuri resolves the issue: here 'kulliyya' simply means the subject is a universal term. Understanding this nuance is crucial to reading classical logic texts, where terms sometimes shift meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the natural proposition
Locate the definition: a proposition where the judgment is on the very nature, not on individuals. Example: 'Human is a species'. Recognize that the subject 'human' is universal, but the judgment applies to the essence, not to each person.
The poet omitted this type because sciences deal with individuals.
2
Understand why the natural proposition is omitted
The passage says it is not used in the sciences. Reason: scientific propositions require applicability to individuals, whereas natural propositions only regard the essence.
This exclusion is standard in the tradition.
3
Parse the shift in meaning of 'kulliyya'
The poet calls the categorical proposition 'kulliyya', but then divides it into quantified and indefinite. This forces 'kulliyya' to mean 'having a universal subject', not 'universally quantified'. The commentator explicitly states this is contrary to common technical usage.
Contrast with 'shakhsiyya' (individual), which has a singular subject.
4
Apply the new understanding to examples
Given a proposition like 'Every human is mortal', note: the subject 'human' is universal, and it is quantified (every). Another: 'Human is a species' has a universal subject but is indefinite (no quantifier). Both would be 'kulliyya' in this context.
This shows why the division into musawwara and muhmala is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنَّهَا تُسَمَّى: «طَبِيعِيَّةٌ»؛ لِوُقُوعِ الْحُكْمِ فِيهَا عَلَى نَفْسِ الطَّبِيعَةِ وَالْحَقِيقَةِ، وَتَرَكَ النَّاظِمُ هَذَا الْقِسْمَ تَبَعًا لِغَيْرِهِ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ لَيْسَ مِنَ الْقَضَايَا الْمُسْتَعْمَلَةِ فِي الْعُلُومِ. فَقَوْلُ النَّاظِمِ: وَالثَّانِي أَيِ الْحَمْلِيَّةُ، وَقَدَّمَهَا عَلَى الشَّرْطِيَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا مِنْهَا بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمُفْرَدِ مِنَ الْمُرَكَّبِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: «كُلِّيَّةٌ» قَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ الْكُلِّيَّةَ عِنْدَهُمْ هِيَ الْقَضِيَّةُ الْمَحْكُومُ فِيهَا عَلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ، لَكِنْ لَا يَصِحُّ أَنْ يَكُونَ هَذَا مُرَادُهُ هُنَا؛ لِأَنَّ تَقْسِيمَهَا إِلَى مُسَوَّرٍ وَمُهْمَلٍ يَمْنَعُهُ، وَإِنَّمَا مُرَادُهُ بِالْكُلِّيَّةِ مَا مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلِّيٌّ؛ سُوِّرَتْ أَوْ لَا، وَلِذَا قَابَلَهَا بِالشَّخْصِيَّةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ خِلَافَ الْاِصْطِلَاحِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: وَالْأَوَّلُ أَيِ الَّتِي مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلِّيٌّ: إِمَّا مُسَوَّرَةٌ أَوْ مُهْمَلَةٌ. إِلَخْ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَأَرْبَعٌ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرَى إِمَّا بِكُلِّ أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ، أَوْ بِلَا شَيْءٍ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضٌ أَوْ شِبْهُ جَلَا) قَدْ عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ السُّوْرَ قِسْمَانِ: كُلِّيٌّ، وَهُوَ مَا دَلَّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ الْحُكْمِ لِجَمِيعِ الْأَفْرَادِ؛ وَجُزْئِيٌّ، وَهُوَ مَا دَلَّ عَلَى تَبْعِيضِهِ. وَالْقَضِيَّةُ مَعَ كُلٍّ مِنْهُمَا: إِمَّا مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، فَلِذَا انْقَسَمَ السُّوْرُ إِلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْسَامٍ، وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ أَمْثِلَتَهَا. قَوْلُ النَّاظِمِ: (وَأَرْبَعٌ أَقْسَامُهُ ... إِلَخْ) حَذَفَ التَّاءَ مِنْ «أَرْبَعَةٍ»، وَهُوَ عَدَدٌ مَذْكُورٌ مَعَ مَعْدُودِهِ جَائِزٌ بِقِلَّةٍ وَلَيْسَ بِضَرُورَةٍ.
Thus it is called 'natural' (tabi'iyya) because the judgment in it occurs on the very nature and reality. And the author (al-Sullam) omitted this division following others, because it is not among the propositions used in the sciences. So the poet's statement: 'And the second'—i.e., the categorical [proposition], and he presented it before the conditional because it is in relation to it like a simple term to a compound. And his statement: 'universal' (kulliyya) — it is known that 'universal' in their terminology is the proposition in which a judgment is made about every individual, but this cannot be his intended meaning here, because its division into quantified and indefinite prevents that. Rather, by 'universal' he means that whose subject is universal, whether quantified or not, and therefore he contrasted it with 'individual' (shakhsiyya), even though it is contrary to the technical usage. And his statement: 'And the first'—i.e., that whose subject is universal: either quantified (musawwara) or indefinite (muhmala), etc. His statement: 'And four are its divisions when it runs, either with 'every' or with 'some', or with nothing, and not 'some' or its likeness...' You have known that the quantifier is of two types: universal, which indicates generalization of the judgment to all individuals; and particular, which indicates particularization of it. And the proposition with each of them is either affirmative or negative. Therefore the quantifier is divided into four divisions, and he mentioned their examples. The poet's statement: ('And four are its divisions...') — he omitted the 'ta'' from 'arba'a', and it is a number mentioned with its counted noun, permissible but rare, and not a poetic necessity.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage explains the natural proposition (tabi'iyya) and then clarifies the poet's classification of categorical propositions. First, the natural proposition is defined as one where the judgment is on the essence itself, not on individuals, e.g., 'Human is a species'. The poet omitted it because it is not used in the sciences, which deal with individuals. Next, the commentator explains the poet's assertion that the categorical proposition is 'universal' (kulliyya). He cautions that here 'universal' does not mean a proposition judging every individual; rather it means the subject is a universal term, whether quantified or not. It is contrasted with 'individual' (shakhsiyya) which has a singular subject. Then the poet's line about the quantifier is parsed: the quantifier is two types (universal and particular), and each can accompany an affirmative or negative proposition, giving four divisions. The poet mentions these four and gives examples (not quoted here). Finally, a grammatical note: the poet used 'arba'' without the feminine 'ta'' (arba'atun) for the number, which is permissible but rare.

طبيعية
كلية
شخصية

C) Because the poet did not know the standard terminology.
D) Because all categorical propositions have universal subjects.

Key Takeaway
In this text, 'kulliyya' means a proposition with a universal subject, whether quantified or not, distinct from 'shakhsiyya' (individual subject).

The Four Quantifiers of Categorical Propositions
Author distinguishes natural proposition, explains poet's classification, and lists four quantifier types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four quantifiers (sur) and identify the quantifier of any given categorical proposition.

Core Idea
Quantifiers are universal or particular, each paired with affirmative or negative, yielding four types.

After clarifying that the categorical proposition has a universal subject, the poet divides it into those that have a quantifier (sūr) and those that are indefinite. The quantifier itself is of two types: universal (kullī) and particular (juz'ī). When combined with the quality of the proposition (affirmative or negative), we get four standard forms: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. This passage explains the poet's concise line covering these four.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of quantifier
The passage states: 'the quantifier is of two types: universal, which indicates generalization of the judgment to all individuals; and particular, which indicates particularization of it.' Recognize that 'universal quantifier' words include 'every', 'all', 'none' (in negative), and 'particular quantifier' words include 'some', 'there exists', etc.
These are the standard logical quantifiers.
2
Combine each quantifier with affirmative and negative
A proposition can be affirmative or negative. So with universal quantifier we get universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every F is G') and universal negative (e.g., 'No F is G'). With particular quantifier we get particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some F is G') and particular negative (e.g., 'Some F is not G'). This yields four divisions.
The poet alludes to these in the line: 'either with 'every' or with 'some', or with nothing, and not 'some' or its likeness…'
3
Parse the poet's line
The poet says: 'And four are its divisions when it runs, either with 'every' or with 'some', or with nothing, and not 'some' or its likeness.' The phrase 'or with nothing' refers to indefinite propositions (no quantifier). The last part 'and not 'some' or its likeness' ensures the 'nothing' is not mistaken for the particular negative quantifier.
This parsing exercise helps read the poem correctly.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Take Quranic or fiqh examples: 'Every intoxicant is haram' (universal affirmative), 'No believer is a disbeliever' (universal negative), 'Some animals are halal' (particular affirmative), 'Some transactions are not valid' (particular negative). Identify the quantifier and quality.
These examples ground the abstract classification in real sacred texts.

Source Text

فَإِنَّهَا تُسَمَّى: «طَبِيعِيَّةٌ»؛ لِوُقُوعِ الْحُكْمِ فِيهَا عَلَى نَفْسِ الطَّبِيعَةِ وَالْحَقِيقَةِ، وَتَرَكَ النَّاظِمُ هَذَا الْقِسْمَ تَبَعًا لِغَيْرِهِ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ لَيْسَ مِنَ الْقَضَايَا الْمُسْتَعْمَلَةِ فِي الْعُلُومِ. فَقَوْلُ النَّاظِمِ: وَالثَّانِي أَيِ الْحَمْلِيَّةُ، وَقَدَّمَهَا عَلَى الشَّرْطِيَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا مِنْهَا بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمُفْرَدِ مِنَ الْمُرَكَّبِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: «كُلِّيَّةٌ» قَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ الْكُلِّيَّةَ عِنْدَهُمْ هِيَ الْقَضِيَّةُ الْمَحْكُومُ فِيهَا عَلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ، لَكِنْ لَا يَصِحُّ أَنْ يَكُونَ هَذَا مُرَادُهُ هُنَا؛ لِأَنَّ تَقْسِيمَهَا إِلَى مُسَوَّرٍ وَمُهْمَلٍ يَمْنَعُهُ، وَإِنَّمَا مُرَادُهُ بِالْكُلِّيَّةِ مَا مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلِّيٌّ؛ سُوِّرَتْ أَوْ لَا، وَلِذَا قَابَلَهَا بِالشَّخْصِيَّةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ خِلَافَ الْاِصْطِلَاحِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: وَالْأَوَّلُ أَيِ الَّتِي مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلِّيٌّ: إِمَّا مُسَوَّرَةٌ أَوْ مُهْمَلَةٌ. إِلَخْ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَأَرْبَعٌ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرَى إِمَّا بِكُلِّ أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ، أَوْ بِلَا شَيْءٍ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضٌ أَوْ شِبْهُ جَلَا) قَدْ عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ السُّوْرَ قِسْمَانِ: كُلِّيٌّ، وَهُوَ مَا دَلَّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ الْحُكْمِ لِجَمِيعِ الْأَفْرَادِ؛ وَجُزْئِيٌّ، وَهُوَ مَا دَلَّ عَلَى تَبْعِيضِهِ. وَالْقَضِيَّةُ مَعَ كُلٍّ مِنْهُمَا: إِمَّا مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، فَلِذَا انْقَسَمَ السُّوْرُ إِلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْسَامٍ، وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ أَمْثِلَتَهَا. قَوْلُ النَّاظِمِ: (وَأَرْبَعٌ أَقْسَامُهُ ... إِلَخْ) حَذَفَ التَّاءَ مِنْ «أَرْبَعَةٍ»، وَهُوَ عَدَدٌ مَذْكُورٌ مَعَ مَعْدُودِهِ جَائِزٌ بِقِلَّةٍ وَلَيْسَ بِضَرُورَةٍ.
Thus it is called 'natural' (tabi'iyya) because the judgment in it occurs on the very nature and reality. And the author (al-Sullam) omitted this division following others, because it is not among the propositions used in the sciences. So the poet's statement: 'And the second'—i.e., the categorical [proposition], and he presented it before the conditional because it is in relation to it like a simple term to a compound. And his statement: 'universal' (kulliyya) — it is known that 'universal' in their terminology is the proposition in which a judgment is made about every individual, but this cannot be his intended meaning here, because its division into quantified and indefinite prevents that. Rather, by 'universal' he means that whose subject is universal, whether quantified or not, and therefore he contrasted it with 'individual' (shakhsiyya), even though it is contrary to the technical usage. And his statement: 'And the first'—i.e., that whose subject is universal: either quantified (musawwara) or indefinite (muhmala), etc. His statement: 'And four are its divisions when it runs, either with 'every' or with 'some', or with nothing, and not 'some' or its likeness...' You have known that the quantifier is of two types: universal, which indicates generalization of the judgment to all individuals; and particular, which indicates particularization of it. And the proposition with each of them is either affirmative or negative. Therefore the quantifier is divided into four divisions, and he mentioned their examples. The poet's statement: ('And four are its divisions...') — he omitted the 'ta'' from 'arba'a', and it is a number mentioned with its counted noun, permissible but rare, and not a poetic necessity.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage explains the natural proposition (tabi'iyya) and then clarifies the poet's classification of categorical propositions. First, the natural proposition is defined as one where the judgment is on the essence itself, not on individuals, e.g., 'Human is a species'. The poet omitted it because it is not used in the sciences, which deal with individuals. Next, the commentator explains the poet's assertion that the categorical proposition is 'universal' (kulliyya). He cautions that here 'universal' does not mean a proposition judging every individual; rather it means the subject is a universal term, whether quantified or not. It is contrasted with 'individual' (shakhsiyya) which has a singular subject. Then the poet's line about the quantifier is parsed: the quantifier is two types (universal and particular), and each can accompany an affirmative or negative proposition, giving four divisions. The poet mentions these four and gives examples (not quoted here). Finally, a grammatical note: the poet used 'arba'' without the feminine 'ta'' (arba'atun) for the number, which is permissible but rare.

The Natural Proposition and Its Omission from Sciences
Commentator explains why natural proposition is omitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a natural proposition and explain why it is excluded from scientific discourse.

Core Idea
Natural propositions judge the essence, not the individuals.

The commentator explains that the poet omitted the natural proposition because it is not used in the sciences. Understanding why helps grasp which propositions are relevant in logical and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the natural proposition
A natural proposition is one where the judgment applies to the nature or essence itself, e.g., 'Human is a species.' Notice that it does not involve individuals.
The term 'tabi'iyya' comes from 'tabi'a' meaning nature.
2
Distinguish from categorical propositions
Categorical propositions predicate something of a subject that is either universal or particular. Natural propositions are a subtype of categorical but are set apart.
3
Determine reason for omission
The commentator states it is omitted because it is not among the propositions used in the sciences. Scientific disciplines deal with individuals and quantifiable statements.
4
Check the analogy with categorical and conditional
The commentator adds that the categorical proposition is to the conditional as a simple term is to a compound, highlighting the primacy of categoricals.
This reinforces why the poet prioritizes categoricals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنَّهَا تُسَمَّى: «طَبِيعِيَّةٌ ؛ لِوُقُوع الحُكْم فِيهَا عَلَى نَفْسِ الطَّبِيعَةِ وَالحَقِيقَةِ، وَتَرَكَ النَّاظِمُ هَذَا القِسْمَ تبَعاً لِغَيْرِهِ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ لَيْسَ مِنَ القَضَايَا المُسْتَعْمَلَةِ فِي العُلُومِ. فَقَوْلُ النَّاظِمِ : وَالثَّانِي أَي الحَمْلِيَّةُ، وَقَدَّمَهَا عَلَى الشَّرْطِيَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا مِنْهَا بِمَنْزِلَةِ المُفْرَدِ مِنَ المُرَكَّبِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : «كُلِّيَّة» قَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ الكُلِّيَّةَ عِنْدَهُمْ هِيَ القَضِيَّةُ المَحْكُومُ فِيهَا عَلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ، لَكِنْ لَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ هَذَا مُرَادُهُ هُنَا ؛ لِأَنَّ تَقْسِيمَهَا إِلَى مُسَوَّرٍ وَمُهْمَلٍ يَمْنَعُهُ، وَإِنَّمَا مُرَادُهُ بِالكُلِّيَّةِ مَا مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلّيّ؛ سُوِّرَتْ أَوْ لَا ، وَلِذَا قَابَلَهَا بِالشَّخْصِيَّةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ خِلَافَ الإصْطلاحِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : وَالأَوَّلُ أَي : الَّتِي مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلّيّ : إِمَّا مُسَوَّرَةٌ , أَوْ مُهْمَلَةٌ .
So it is called 'natural' because the judgment in it applies to the nature and reality itself. The author omitted this type following others, because it is not among the propositions used in the sciences. So the author's statement: 'the second' means the categorical, and he gave it priority over the conditional because it is to it like a single term to a compound. And his statement: 'universal' – it is known that 'universal' according to them is the proposition judged about every individual, but it is not correct that this is his intent here, because the division into quantified and unquantified prevents it. Rather, his intent by 'universal' is that whose subject is universal, whether quantified or not. That is why he contrasted it with 'particular', even if it is contrary to terminology. And his statement: 'the first' means that whose subject is universal: either quantified or unquantified, etc.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the commentator on the poet's terms. First, the commentator explains the natural proposition (tabi'iyya), where judgment is on the essence itself (e.g., 'Human is a species'). The author omitted it because it is not used in the sciences. The commentator then notes that the second type is the categorical (hamliyya), prioritized because it functions like a simple term within a compound. Next, he clarifies the meaning of 'kulliyya': in standard usage it denotes a proposition judged about every individual, but that cannot be meant here since the division into quantified (musawwara) and unquantified (muhmala) would be impossible. Instead, 'kulliyya' here means a proposition whose subject is a universal (kulli), whether quantified or not. That is why it is contrasted with 'shakhsiyya' (particular). Finally, the first type is that with a universal subject, which is either quantified or unquantified (the latter being the muhmala proposition without explicit quantifier). This clarification prevents confusion between the technical term for universal quantifier and the broader notion of a universal subject.

The Meaning of 'Kulliyya': Universal Subject vs Universal Quantifier
Commentator clarifies the meaning of 'kulliyya' as universal subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the context-specific meaning of 'kulliyya' (universal subject) from the standard meaning (universal quantifier) and explain the division into quantified and unquantified propositions.

Core Idea
'Kulliyya' here means a proposition with a universal subject, not necessarily quantified.

The commentator resolves a terminological issue: the poet uses 'kulliyya' to mean a proposition whose subject is a universal, not the usual 'universal proposition' that is quantified. This clarification prevents confusion and allows a logical division.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard meaning of 'kulliyya'
In standard Mantiq, 'kulliyya' refers to a proposition quantified with 'kull' (every), e.g., 'Every human is mortal.'
2
Identify the contradiction in context
The commentator notes that if 'kulliyya' meant universally quantified, then dividing it into quantified (musawwara) and unquantified (muhmala) would be impossible because the unquantified would already be excluded.
This logical inconsistency forces a reinterpretation.
3
Grasp the author's intended meaning
The author's 'kulliyya' means any proposition whose subject is a universal (kulli), regardless of whether a quantifier is present. This allows the division.
The subject being universal is the key.
4
Contrast with 'shakhsiyya'
The contrast is with 'shakhsiyya' (particular), where the subject is a particular (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). This confirms that the focus is on the subject's nature.
5
Apply the division
Understanding that a proposition with a universal subject can be either musawwara (with explicit quantifier) or muhmala (unquantified) completes the logical picture.

Source Text

The Four Divisions of the Quantifier
Commentator explains the four-fold division of the quantifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition into one of the four quantifier types based on its quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative).

Core Idea
Quantifier splits into four: universal/particular × affirmative/negative.

In this lesson, we examine how the quantifier (sawr) in categorical propositions is divided into universal and particular, and how each combines with the quality of the proposition (affirmative or negative) to yield four distinct types. This classification is the backbone of syllogistic logic used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary quantifier types
Recognize that the quantifier is either universal (kulli), meaning the judgment applies to all individuals, or particular (juz'i), meaning it applies to some individuals. Examples: 'every' and 'some'.
2
Understand the quality of the proposition
Each quantified proposition is either affirmative (positive) or negative (negative). The quality indicates whether the predicate is affirmed or denied of the subject.
3
Combine quantity and quality
By pairing the two quantifier types with the two qualities, we obtain four divisions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
4
Classify example propositions
Take a proposition from Islamic law or theology, identify its quantifier (if present) and quality, and determine which of the four types it belongs to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَأَرْبَعٌ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرَى إِمَّا بِكُلِّ أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ، أَوْ بِلَا شَيْءٍ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ أَوْ شِبْهِ جَلَا قَدْ عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ السُّوْرَ قِسْمَانِ : كُلِّيَّ، وَهُوَ : مَا دَلَّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ الحُكْمِ لِجَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ . وَجُزْئِيٌّ ، وَهُوَ : مَا دَلَّ عَلَى تَبْعِيضِهِ . وَالقَضِيَّةُ مَعَ كُلٍّ مِنْهُمَا: إِمَّا مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، فَلِذَا انْقَسَمَ السُّوْرُ إِلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْسَامٍ، وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ أَمْئِلَتَهَا .
His statement: 'and four are its divisions insofar as it occurs either with 'every', or with 'some', or with nothing, and not some or like [garbled]' – you have known that the quantifier is of two types: universal, which signifies the generalization of the judgment to all individuals; and particular, which signifies the particularization of it. And the proposition with each of them is either affirmative or negative. Thus the quantifier is divided into four divisions, and he has mentioned their examples.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poet's line about the four divisions of the quantifier. The commentator first explains that the quantifier (sawr) has two primary types: universal (kulli), which means the judgment applies to all individuals, and particular (juz'i), which means the judgment applies to some individuals. Then he observes that a proposition with each quantifier can be either affirmative (mujaba) or negative (saliba). Therefore, by combining the two quantifier types with the two qualities, we obtain four divisions. The commentator notes that the author has already provided examples for each of these four. This classification is foundational for understanding quantified propositions in logic, used extensively in syllogistic reasoning. For instance, 'Every human is mortal' is a universal affirmative, while 'Some animal is black' is particular affirmative.

Key Terms
سور
كلي
جزئي
موجبة
سالبة

Checkpoint

D) Eight

Key Takeaway
The quantifier is divided into four types based on quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative), which are essential for classifying categorical propositions.

Construct a universal affirmative proposition about the obligation of prayer, and then convert it into a particular negative by changing the quantifier and quality.

Quantifier Words for Categorical Propositions
Author lists concrete words for each quantifier type with Quranic and logical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier word in any categorical proposition and classify it as universal affirmative, universal negative, or particular affirmative.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition's quantifier reveals its quantity and quality.

In this lesson, we examine the concrete words that serve as quantifiers (sawr) for categorical propositions in Islamic logic. The author lists the specific terms used for each quantifier type, along with Quranic and theological examples. Understanding these quantifier words is essential for correctly interpreting propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish universal affirmative quantifiers
Look for words like 'kull' (distributive), 'jami‘', and the definite article 'al-istighraqiyya'. They assert a predicate for every individual of the subject.
Avoid confusing distributive 'kull' with collective usage.
2
Recognize universal negative quantifiers
Words like 'la shay' and 'la wahid' negate the predicate for every individual. Also indefinite nouns in negative contexts can indicate universality.
Indefinite nouns in negation may be explicit (e.g., 'ahad') or apparent (e.g., 'rajul').
3
Identify particular affirmative quantifiers
Words like 'ba‘d' and 'wahid' (meaning 'a certain') indicate the predicate applies to some but not all individuals. Indefinite nouns in affirmative also usually signal particularity.
Indefinite nouns in affirmative can sometimes be metaphorical for universality (e.g., 'tamratun khayrun min jaradat'in').
4
Handle indefinite nouns (nakira) carefully
In affirmative, nakira normally indicates particularity; in negative, it can be explicit or apparent for universality. Always check context.
The commentator explains when nakira in negation is explicit (specialized to negation) or apparent.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Identify quantifiers in Quranic verses (e.g., 'kullu nafsin dha'iqatu al-mawt' – universal affirmative) and in theological statements (e.g., 'la shay'a min al-'alami bi qadimin' – universal negative).
Practice with the examples given in the passage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اللَّهِ ، وَ: «مَا لِبَاغِ مِنْ مَفَرِّه . وَسُورُ الإِيْجَابِ الجُزْئِيِّ : بَعْضُ وَ: «وَاحِدُ»، وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا، وَمِنْهُ النَّكِرَةُ فِي الإِثْبَاتِ مُطْلَقاً ؛ نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ العُصاةِ مَغْفُورٌ لَهُ ، وَ وَاحِدٌ مِنَ الأَنْبِيَاءِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ يَشْفَعُ فِي جَمِيعِ الخَلْقِ ، وَ اثْنَانِ مِنَ القُضَاةِ فِي النَّارِهِ، وَ: «عَشْرَةٌ مِنَ الصَّحَابَةِ مُبَشِّرُونَ بِالجَنَّةِ . قوله : (وَلَفْظُ جَمِيعَ وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا ) أي : مِن كلِّ ما يدلُّ على إثبات الحكم لكل فرد؛ نحو . جاء القوم قاطبة، أو جميعاً، أو عامة أو طرًّا ، أو كافَّةً، أو أجمعون، وسائر ألفاظ العموم ك: «أل» الاستغراقيَّة. قوله : «لَا شَيْءَ وَ لَا وَاحِدَ، وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا أي: مِن كلِّ ما دل على عموم السَّلب؛ نحو لا إنسان ولا واحد من كلّ نكرة وقعت في سياق النفي وهي إما نص في العموم، وإمَّا ظاهرةٌ ؛ فالأوَّل إذا كانت مختصَّةً بالنَّفي؛ نحو: ما جاءني أحد»، أو مع «من» ظاهرة؛ نحو: ما لباغ من مفر»، أو مقدَّرة؛ نحو: «لا إله إلا الله» و «لا أحد أغير من الله، والثاني وهي ما إذا كانت ظاهرةً فيه، فتحمل عليه إلا لقرينة، ومثال ما إذا كانت في غيرهما نحو : «ما جاءني رجل». قوله : (وَمِنْهُ النَّكِرَةُ فِي الإِثْبَاتِ مُطْلَقاً) أي: مفردةً كانت ؛ نحو: ﴿وَجَاءَ رَجُلٌ مِّنْ أَقْصَ الْمَدِينَةِ الله [القصص: ٢٠]، أو جمعاً ؛ نحو: ﴿وَقَالَ نِسْوَةٌ فِي الْمَدِينَةِ ﴾ [يوسف: ٣٠]. وقد تستعمل النكرة المثبتة في العموم مجازاً كثيراً في المبتدأ ؛ نحو: «تمرة خير من جرادة»، وقليلاً في غيره ؛ نحو: عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا قَدَّمَتْ [الانفطار: ٥]، وفي المقامات: (1) بنحوه أخرجه البخاري (٥٢٢٠) ، ومسلم (٢٧٦٠)، من حديث عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله تعالى عنه .
The quantifier of universal affirmative: the word 'kull' in its individual sense, not collective sense, and the word 'jami'' and what is in their meaning, such as 'Every soul will taste death' [Quran 3:185], and 'Everything besides Allah is temporally originated', and from that the definite article 'al' for generality, such as 'And man was created weak' [Quran 4:28]. And the quantifier of universal negative: 'la shay' (nothing) and 'la wahid' (not one), and what is in their meaning, such as 'Nothing of the world is eternal', and 'No one of the contingent is independent of the free agent', and from it 'There is no god except Allah' and 'No one is more jealous than Allah' and 'No wrongdoer has any escape'. And the quantifier of particular affirmative: 'ba'd' (some) and 'wahid' (one), and what is in their meaning, and from it the indefinite noun in affirmative absolutely, such as 'Some sinners are forgiven', and 'One of the prophets, peace be upon them, intercedes for all creation', and 'Two judges are in the Fire', and 'Ten Companions are given glad tidings of Paradise'. His statement: 'and the word jami' and what is in their meaning' means everything that indicates affirmation of the judgment for every individual, like 'the people came entirely' or 'all' or 'generally' or 'entirely' or 'all together' or 'all', and all words of generality like the definite article 'al'. His statement: 'la shay' and la wahid and what is in their meaning' means everything that indicates universal negation, like 'no human' and 'not one' from every indefinite noun that occurs in a context of negation, and it is either explicit in generality or apparent; the first is when it is specialized to negation, like 'No one came to me', or with apparent 'min' like 'No escape for a wrongdoer', or implied like 'There is no god except Allah' and 'No one is more jealous than Allah'; the second is when it is apparent in it, so it is interpreted as such unless there is a contrary indicator. His statement: 'and from it the indefinite in affirmative absolutely' means whether it is singular, like 'And there came a man from the far end of the city' [Quran 28:20], or plural like 'And women said in the city' [Quran 12:30]. And sometimes the indefinite positive is used metaphorically for universality, frequently in the subject, like 'A date is better than a locust', and rarely elsewhere, like 'Every soul knows what it has sent forth' [Quran 82:5]. And in some contexts, similar to what is narrated by Bukhari and Muslim from Ibn Mas'ud.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage enumerates the concrete words used for each of the four quantifier types. First, universal affirmative quantifiers include the word 'kull' (each/every) in the distributive sense (not collective), 'jami'' (all), and equivalents like the definite article 'al' for generality, with Quranic examples (e.g., 'Every soul will taste death'). Second, universal negative quantifiers include 'la shay' (nothing) and 'la wahid' (not one), with examples from theology (e.g., 'Nothing of the world is eternal') and the Shahada. Third, particular affirmative quantifiers include 'ba'd' (some) and 'wahid' (a certain one), and also the indefinite noun (nakira) used in an affirmative context. The commentator then elaborates: the indefinite noun in affirmative is understood as particular unless a contextual indicator suggests universality (metaphorically). He explains that the indefinite noun in negation can be explicit ('anyone') or apparent ('a man'), and gives examples from Quran and Hadith. This detailed listing helps the student identify quantifiers in actual propositions across Islamic sciences.

Universal Quantifiers: Affirmative and Negative
Author defines universal affirmative and negative quantifiers with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier (sūr) of a universal affirmative or universal negative proposition and distinguish the distributive from the collective meaning of 'kull'.

Core Idea
The universal quantifier tells you whether the judgment applies to every individual or is negated from every individual.

Quantifiers (sūr) are the particles that determine how many individuals of the subject are included in a proposition. In Islamic logic, distinguishing universal from particular propositions is essential for constructing valid syllogisms and understanding Quranic and kalām arguments. This passage defines the two universal quantifiers—affirmative and negative—with clear linguistic and Quranic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier word
Look for words like 'kull' (كل), 'jami'' (جميع), or the definite article 'al' for universal affirmative; for universal negative, look for 'la shay' (لا شيء) or 'la wahid' (لا واحد) or any indefinite noun in the context of negation.
The quantifier is usually at the beginning of the proposition or attached to the subject.
2
Determine quality: affirmative or negative
Check whether the quantifier asserts or denies the predicate of the subject. Affirmative quantifiers like 'kull' assert, while negative quantifiers like 'la shay' deny.
This corresponds to the ījāb/salb distinction.
3
Verify distributive meaning for 'kull'
Ensure that 'kull' is used distributively (ifrādī) meaning each individual separately, not collectively (majmūʿī) meaning the group as a whole. The passage explicitly warns against the collective interpretation.
Example: 'Every soul shall taste death' means each soul individually.
4
Recognize synonyms and particles of generality
For affirmative, words like 'qāṭibatan', 'ʿāmmatan', 'kaffatan' etc. indicate universal affirmation. For negative, indefinite nouns like 'aḥad' in negation context indicate universal negation.
The commentary lists many such words; they all function as universal quantifiers.
5
Apply to Quranic examples
Test your understanding by parsing the examples: 'Kullu nafsin dhā'iqatu l-mawt' is universal affirmative; 'Lā shay'a mina l-ʿālami biqadīm' is universal negative.
These examples come directly from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَسُورُ الإِيجَابِ الكُلِّيِّ : لَفْظُ كُلِّ الإِفْرَادِيُّ، لَا المَجْمُوعِيُّ ، وَلَفْظُ جَمِيعَ وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا ، نَحْوُ : كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ [آل عمران (۱۸۵]، وَ جَمِيعُ مَا سِوَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى حَادِتٌ، وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ «أَلْ» الِاسْتِغْرَاقِيَّةُ ، نَحْوُ : ﴿وَخُلِقَ الإِنسَانُ ضَعِيفًا [النساء: ۲۸] .
قوله : (وَلَفْظُ جَمِيعَ وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا ) أي : مِن كلِّ ما يدلُّ على إثبات الحكم لكل فرد؛ نحو: جاء القوم قاطبة، أو جميعاً، أو عامة أو طرًّا ، أو كافَّةً، أو أجمعون، وسائر ألفاظ العموم ك: «أل» الاستغراقيَّة.
وَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الكُلّيّ : لَا شَيْءٍ وَ لَا وَاحِدَ، وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ العَالَمِ بِقَدِيمِ»، وَ: «لَا وَاحِدَ مِنَ الجَائِزِ بِغَنِيٍّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ المُخْتَارِ، وَمِنْهُ: «لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ»، وَ: «لَا أَحَدٌ أَغْيَرُ مِنَ اللَّهِ»، وَ: «مَا لِبَاغِ مِنْ مَفَرِّ».
قوله : «لَا شَيْءَ وَ لَا وَاحِدَ، وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا أي: مِن كلِّ ما دل على عموم السَّلب؛ نحو لا إنسان ولا واحد من كلّ نكرة وقعت في سياق النفي وهي إما نص في العموم، وإمَّا ظاهرةٌ ؛ فالأوَّل إذا كانت مختصَّةً بالنَّفي؛ نحو: ما جاءني أحد»، أو مع «من» ظاهرة؛ نحو: ما لباغ من مفر»، أو مقدَّرة؛ نحو: «لا إله إلا الله» و «لا أحد أغير من الله، والثاني وهي ما إذا كانت ظاهرةً فيه، فتحمل عليه إلا لقرينة، ومثال ما إذا كانت في غيرهما نحو : «ما جاءني رجل».
The quantifier of universal affirmative is the word 'kull' (every) in the distributive sense, not the collective sense, and the word 'jami'' (all) and what is similar in meaning to them, like: 'Every soul shall taste death' [Al-Imran 185], and 'All besides Allah the Exalted is originated', and among that is the 'al' of generality, like: 'And man was created weak' [An-Nisa 28].
His statement: 'and the word 'jami'' and what is similar in meaning to them' means: from everything that indicates the affirmation of the judgment for every individual, like: 'the people came all together, or all, or generally, or wholly, or entirely, or all together', and other words of generality like the 'al' of generality.
And the quantifier of universal negative is 'la shay' (nothing) and 'la wahid' (not one) and what is similar in meaning to them, like: 'Nothing of the world is eternal', and 'No one of that which is possible is independent of the efficient cause who chooses', and from it: 'There is no god but Allah', and 'No one is more jealous than Allah', and 'There is no escape for the oppressor'.
His statement: 'la shay' and 'la wahid' and what is similar in meaning to them' means: from everything that indicates the generality of negation, like 'no human' and 'not one' from every indefinite noun that falls in the context of negation. It is either explicit in generality or apparent. The first is if it is specific to negation, like 'no one came to me', or with an apparent 'min' like 'there is no escape for the oppressor', or implicit like 'There is no god but Allah' and 'No one is more jealous than Allah'. The second is if it is apparent in it, then it is understood as such unless there is a counter-indication, and an example when it is other than those is like 'no man came to me'.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage defines the two universal quantifiers: the universal affirmative and the universal negative. It begins by specifying the quantifier for universal affirmative: the word 'kull' (every) in the distributive (not collective) sense, and the word 'jami'' (all) and their synonyms. Examples are given from the Quran: 'Every soul shall taste death' and 'All besides Allah is originated' (meaning everything other than Allah is created). The definite article 'al' used generically is also included, as in 'And man was created weak' (where 'man' refers to all humans). The commentary expands on these quantifiers, listing other words that also indicate universal affirmation, such as 'all together', 'entirely', etc. Then the universal negative quantifier is defined: 'la shay' (nothing) and 'la wahid' (not one) and their synonyms. Examples include 'Nothing of the world is eternal' and 'No one of that which is possible is independent of the efficient cause'. The commentary explains that these are indefinite nouns in the context of negation, and they are either explicit in indicating universality (like 'no one came to me') or apparent (like 'no man came to me' which is usually understood as universal unless a counter-indication is present). This establishes the foundational terms for classifying propositions by quantity and quality.

كُلِّي
سُور
إِيجَاب
سَلْب

Key Takeaway
The universal quantifier determines the scope of the judgment: universal affirmative asserts the predicate for every individual, universal negative denies it for every individual.

Distinguishing 'Laysa Kullu' from Other Particular Negatives
Defining particular negative quantifiers and their logical behavior

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish 'laysa kullu' from 'laysa ba'du' and 'ba'du laysa' in terms of their primary meaning and entailment, and apply this distinction in analyzing Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
ليس كل negates the universal affirmative; the others directly state particular negation.

In this lesson, we examine the particular negative quantifiers in Islamic logic. The author defines three expressions and clarifies a subtle logical distinction between them. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting negative propositions in Islamic theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three expressions for particular negative
Find the three expressions in the Arabic text: 'ليس كل', 'ليس بعض', and 'بعض ليس'. Note that they are all used for particular negative propositions.
2
Understand the primary meaning of each
According to the passage, 'ليس كل' primarily means the negation of universal affirmation (رفع الإيجاب الكلي). The other two primarily mean particular negation (السلب الجزئي).
Primary meaning = المطابقي
3
Trace the entailments
Recognize that 'ليس كل' entails particular negation (اللزوم), and the other two entail the negation of universal affirmation. This is reversed relationship.
Entailment is logical consequence.
4
Apply to examples
Test the given example: 'ليس كل ميت تأكله الأرض'. Does it mean some dead are not eaten, or all are not? The passage explains that it is ambiguous but conventionally taken as particular.
Consider the Quranic verse 57:23 mentioned in the commentary.

Source Text

وَسُورُ الإِيْجَابِ الجُزْئِيِّ : بَعْضُ وَ: «وَاحِدُ»، وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا، وَمِنْهُ النَّكِرَةُ فِي الإِثْبَاتِ مُطْلَقاً ; نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ العُصاةِ مَغْفُورٌ لَهُ ، وَ وَاحِدٌ مِنَ الأَنْبِيَاءِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ يَشْفَعُ فِي جَمِيعِ الخَلْقِ ، وَ اثْنَانِ مِنَ القُضَاةِ فِي النَّارِهِ، وَ: «عَشْرَةٌ مِنَ الصَّحَابَةِ مُبَشِّرُونَ بِالجَنَّةِ .
قوله : (وَمِنْهُ النَّكِرَةُ فِي الإِثْبَاتِ مُطْلَقاً) أي: مفردةً كانت ; نحو: ﴿وَجَاءَ رَجُلٌ مِّنْ أَقْصَ الْمَدِينَةِ الله [القصص: ٢٠] ، أو جمعاً ; نحو: ﴿وَقَالَ نِسْوَةٌ فِي الْمَدِينَةِ ﴾ [يوسف: ٣٠].
وَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الجُزْئِيِّ : «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ» وَ : «بَعْضُ لَيْسَ» وَ: «لَيْسَ كُلُّ»; نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ النَّاسِ بِمُحَاسَبٍ ، وَ بَعْضُ الذَّنْبِ لَيْسَ بِمَغْفُورٍ»، وَ: «لَيْسَ كُلُّ مَيْتٍ تَأْكُلُهُ الأَرْضُ»، وَ: «مَا جَمِيعُ النَّاسِ بِمُؤْمِنِينَ».
وَفَرَّقُوا بَيْنَ هَذِهِ الأَلْفَاظِ : بِأَنَّ لَيْسَ كُلُّ مَفْهُومُهُ المُطَابِقِيُّ رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الكُلِّيَّ، وَيَلْزَمُهُ السَّلْبُ الجُزْنِيُّ، وَهُوَ سَلْبُ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ البَعْضِ، وَالآخَرَانِ بِالعَكْسِ، فَإِنَّ مَنْهُومَهُمَا المُطَابِقِيَّ هُوَ السَّلْبُ الجُزْنِيُّ ، وَيَلْزَمُهُمَا رَفْعُ الإِيجَابِ الكُلِّيَّ .
قوله : (رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الكُلِّيَّ، وَيَلْزَمُهُ ... إلخ) بيانُهُ : أَنَّا إذا قلنا : «كل حيوان إنسان» كان معناه : ثبوت الإنسانيَّة لكلِّ فردٍ من أفراد الحيوان، وإذا قلنا : ليس كلُّ حَيَوان إنساناً» فقد رفعنا ذلك الحكم ; أي ليست الإنسانيَّة ثابتةٌ لكلِّ فرد من أفراد الحيوان; هذا مدلوله المطابقي صادق بأن تكون الإنسانية ليست ثابتة لشيء من أفراده، وهو السَّلب الكلِّيُّ، أو تكون ثابتة للبعض منسلبة عن البعض، وأيا ما كان يتحقَّق السَّلب الجزئي ; لأنَّه إنِ انسلب الحكم عن الجميع، فقد انسلب عن البعض، وإن انسلب عن البعض وثبت للبعض، فقد انسلب عن البعض أيضاً، فليس كلٌّ يستلزمُ السَّلبَ الجزئي لا محالة ، فكان دالا عليه بالالتزام ولا شكَّ أنَّه يحتمل السَّلبَ الكلّي أيضاً معه، كما قررنا ، لكنَّهم لم يعتبروه فيه، بل اقتصروا على السَّلب الجزئي; أخذاً بالمحقق وتركاً للمشكوك .
وما ذكره السعد في شرح التَّلخيص مِن : أنَّ استعمال ليس كل» في السَّلب الجزئي إنَّما هو أكثري، لا لازم بدليل : ﴿ وَاللهُ لا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ مُخْتَالٍ فَخُورٍ [الحديد: ٢٣]، فسيأتي تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى .
قوله : (فَإِنَّ مَنْهُومَهُمَا المُطَابِقِيَّ هُوَ السَّلْبُ الجُزْنِيُّ أي : لأنَّ معناهما سلب المحمول عن بعض أفراد الموضوع، ويلزمهما : رفع الإيجاب الكلّيّ ; لأنَّه إذا انتفى عن البعض لم يكن ثابتاً لكل فردٍ بالضرورة.
And the quantifier of particular affirmative is 'ba'du' (some) and 'wāhidun' (one) and what is similar in meaning to them, and among that is the indefinite noun in affirmative contexts absolutely, like: 'Some disobedient ones are forgiven', and 'One of the prophets, peace be upon them, intercedes for all creation', and 'Two of the judges are in the Fire', and 'Ten of the Companions are given glad tidings of Paradise'.
His statement: 'and among that is the indefinite noun in affirmative contexts absolutely' means: whether it is singular, like {And a man came from the farthest part of the city} [Al-Qasas 20], or plural, like {And women said in the city} [Yusuf 30].
And the quantifier of particular negative is: 'laysa ba'du' (it is not that some), 'ba'du laysa' (some are not), and 'laysa kullu' (not every); like: 'It is not that some people are held accountable', and 'Some sin is not forgiven', and 'Not every dead person does the earth consume', and 'Not all people are believers'.
And they distinguished between these expressions: that 'laysa kullu' has as its primary meaning the negation of universal affirmation, and it entails particular negation, which is the negation of the predicate from some; and the other two are the opposite, for their primary meaning is particular negation, and they entail the negation of universal affirmation.
His statement: 'the negation of universal affirmation, and it entails... etc.' its explanation: If we say 'Every animal is a human', its meaning is the affirmation of humanity for every individual of animal. And if we say 'Not every animal is a human', we have negated that judgment; i.e., humanity is not established for every individual of animal. This primary meaning is true whether humanity is not established for any of its individuals (universal negation) or is established for some and denied from others. In either case, particular negation is realized, because if the judgment is denied from all, it is denied from some, and if denied from some and established for others, it is also denied from some. So 'laysa kullu' necessarily entails particular negation, so it indicates it by implication. And there is no doubt that it also allows universal negation along with it, as we have established, but they did not consider it in it, rather they restricted it to particular negation, taking the certain and leaving the doubtful.
And what al-Sa'd mentioned in his commentary on al-Talkhīs, that the use of 'laysa kullu' for particular negation is only predominant, not necessary, as evidenced by {And Allah does not love every proud boaster} [Al-Hadid 23], and its verification will come, God willing.
His statement: 'for their primary meaning is particular negation' means: because their meaning is the negation of the predicate from some individuals of the subject, and they entail the negation of universal affirmation, because if it is absent from some, it is not established for every individual necessarily.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage covers the particular quantifiers for both affirmative and negative propositions. It first defines the particular affirmative quantifier: the words 'ba'du' (some) and 'wāhidun' (one), and their synonyms. Examples include 'Some disobedient ones are forgiven' and 'One of the prophets intercedes'. The commentary expands on the use of indefinite nouns in affirmative contexts, giving Quranic examples both singular ('a man came') and plural ('women said'). Then the particular negative quantifier is introduced with three forms: 'laysa ba'du' (it is not that some), 'ba'du laysa' (some are not), and 'laysa kullu' (not every). Examples illustrate each: 'It is not that some people are held accountable', 'Some sin is not forgiven', and 'Not every dead person does the earth consume'.
The author then provides a detailed logical distinction between these three expressions. The key point is that 'laysa kullu' (not every) has as its primary meaning the negation of the universal affirmative (i.e., it is false that every S is P), and it entails the particular negative (some S is not P). In contrast, 'laysa ba'du' and 'ba'du laysa' have as their primary meaning the particular negative (some S is not P) and entail the negation of the universal affirmative. The commentary elaborates using the example 'Every animal is a human' vs 'Not every animal is a human'. When we deny that every animal is human, the statement is true if either no animal is human (universal negation) or only some are not human (particular negation). In both cases, the particular negative holds (some animal is not human), so 'laysa kullu' implies the particular negative. However, logicians conventionally treat 'laysa kullu' as indicating particular negation only, ignoring the possibility of universal negation because it is not certain. The passage also references al-Sa'd al-Taftazani's view that the usage is dominant (aktharī) rather than necessary, citing the Quranic verse 'Allah does not love every proud boaster' as an example where 'every' is negated but it could mean universal negation. This shows the nuanced nature of the quantifier.

Key Takeaway
'Laysa kullu' negates a universal affirmative and implies a particular negative, but is not strictly equivalent to a particular negative itself due to potential universal reading.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Three Quantifiers of Particular Negation
Author explains the three quantifiers and their logical properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Arabic quantifiers for particular negative propositions and distinguish their primary (muṭābiqī) meaning from their necessary entailment (ilzām).

Core Idea
'Laysa kullu' denies universality, not the predicate directly.

In Islamic logic, negative propositions have their own special quantifiers. Three common expressions are 'laysa ba'du', 'ba'du laysa', and 'laysa kullu'. At first glance, they all seem to mean 'not all' or 'some...not', but logicians distinguished between their immediate wording and what logically follows. This lesson will help you read these quantifiers precisely.

Guided Path
1
List the three quantifiers
Extract 'laysa ba'du', 'ba'du laysa', and 'laysa kullu' from the passage. Note that order matters: 'ba'du laysa' has 'ba'du' before the negation, while 'laysa ba'du' has negation before 'ba'du'.
2
Identify the primary meaning (mafhūm muṭābiqī) of each
For 'laysa kullu', the passage says its immediate meaning is 'raf' al-ījāb al-kullī' (denial of universal affirmative). For the other two, it is 'salb juz'ī' (particular negation).
3
Determine the necessary entailment
'Laysa kullu' necessarily entails particular negation; the other two necessarily entail denial of universal affirmative. This is a converse relationship.
4
Test with examples
Apply to the examples: 'laysa kullu maytin ta'kuluhu al-arḍ' means 'Not all dead are eaten by earth' (primary: not all, entails some are not eaten). 'Ba'ḍu al-dhanbi laysa bi-maghfūrin' means 'Some sin is not forgiven' (primary: some not, entails not all sin is forgiven).

وَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الجُزْئِيّ: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ»، وَ «بَعْضُ لَيْسَ»، وَ «لَيْسَ كُلُّ»؛ نَحْوُ: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ النَّاسِ بِمُحَاسَبٍ»، وَ «بَعْضُ الذَّنْبِ لَيْسَ بِمَغْفُورٍ»، وَ «لَيْسَ كُلُّ مَيْتٍ تَأْكُلُهُ الأَرْضُ»، وَ «مَا جَمِيعُ النَّاسِ بِمُؤْمِنِينَ». وَفَرَّقُوا بَيْنَ هَذِهِ الأَلْفَاظِ: بِأَنَّ «لَيْسَ كُلَّ» مَفْهُومُهُ المُطَابِقِيُّ رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الكُلِّيَّ، وَيَلْزَمُهُ السَّلْبُ الجُزْنِيُّ، وَهُوَ سَلْبُ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ البَعْضِ، وَالآخَرَانِ بِالعَكْسِ، فَإِنَّ مَفْهُومَهُمَا المُطَابِقِيَّ هُوَ السَّلْبُ الجُزْنِيُّ، وَيَلْزَمُهُمَا رَفْعُ الإِيجَابِ الكُلِّيَّ. قَالَ السَّعْدُ: وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ «لَيْسَ كُلَّ» وَ «لَيْسَ بَعْضَ» إِنِ اعْتُبِرَا دَاخِلَيْنِ عَلَى نَفْسِ القَضِيَّةِ رَافِعَيْنِ لَهَا، فَالأَوَّلُ رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الكُلّيّ؛ أَيْ فَيَكُونُ سَلْباً جُزْنِيًّا؛ لِأَنَّهُ نَقِيضُ الإِنْجَابِ الكُلّيّ، وَالثَّانِي: رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الجُزْنِيّ؛ أَيْ: فَيَكُونُ سَلْباً كُلِّيًّا؛ لِأَنَّهُ نَقِيضُ الإِيجَابِ الجُزْئيّ. وَإِنِ اعْتُبِرَا بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى المَحْمُولِ؛ أَيْ: بِأَنْ أُريدَ بِهِمَا سَلْبَ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ المَوْضُوعِ، لَا سَلْبَ القَضِيَّةِ نَفْسِهَا كَمَا فِي الوَجْهِ الأَوَّلِ، فَالأَوَّلُ سَلْبٌ كُلِّيٌّ، وَالثَّانِي سَلْبٌ جُزْئِيٌّ. فَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا يَكُونُ كُلِّيَّا عَلَى تَقْدِيرٍ، وَجُزْيْنًا عَلَى تَقْدِيرٍ، وَإِنَّمَا عُدَّا مِنْ أَسْوَارِ الجُزْنِيَّةِ؛ لأَنَّ الجُزْئِيَّ صَادِقٌ فِي ضُمْنِ الكُلِّيِّ، فَأَخَذُوا المُحَقَّقَ وَتَرَكُوا المَشْكُوكَ. وَالفَرْقُ بَيْنَ «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ» وَ «بَعْضُ لَيْسَ»: أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ قَدْ يُسْتَعْمَلُ لِلسَّلْبِ الكُلّي؛ نَحْوُ: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ بِحَجَرٍ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لِإِبْهَامِهِ.
And the quantifiers of particular negation: 'Not some', and 'Some not', and 'Not all'; such as: 'Not some people are reckonable', and 'Some sin is not forgiven', and 'Not all dead does the earth consume', and 'Not all people are believers'. And they distinguished between these expressions: that 'Not all' has as its immediate meaning the lifting of universal affirmation, and it necessarily entails particular negation, which is the negation of the predicate from some; and the other two are the opposite, for their immediate meaning is particular negation, and they necessarily entail the lifting of universal affirmation. Al-Sa'd said: The verification is that 'Not all' and 'Not some' if they are considered as entering upon the proposition itself, lifting it, then the first is the lifting of universal affirmation; i.e. it becomes particular negation because it is the contradictory of universal affirmation; and the second is the lifting of particular affirmation; i.e. it becomes universal negation because it is the contradictory of particular affirmation. And if they are considered with respect to the predicate, i.e. intending by them the negation of the predicate from the subject, not the negation of the proposition itself as in the first way, then the first is universal negation, and the second is particular negation. So each of them is universal in one estimation and particular in another. They are only counted among the quantifiers of particularity because the particular is true within the universal, so they took the certain and left the doubtful. And the difference between 'Not some' and 'Some not' is that the former may be used for universal negation; such as: 'Not some animal is a stone'; because of its ambiguity.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the quantifiers (sawar) used for particular negative propositions. It begins by listing three expressions: 'laysa ba'du' (not some), 'ba'du laysa' (some not), and 'laysa kullu' (not all). Examples are given from everyday Arabic, such as 'Not some people are reckonable' and 'Some sin is not forgiven'. The author then explains the distinction made by logicians between these expressions in terms of their primary (mutabiqi) meaning and their necessary entailment (iltizam). For 'laysa kullu', the primary meaning is the lifting of universal affirmation (i.e., denying that the predicate applies to all individuals), and it necessarily entails particular negation (that the predicate is denied of some). For the other two, the situation is reversed: their primary meaning is particular negation, and they necessarily entail the lifting of universal affirmation. The passage then quotes al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) who provides a more detailed analysis. He says that if we consider 'laysa kullu' and 'laysa ba'du' as entering upon the proposition itself and denying it (i.e., as negations of the whole proposition), then 'laysa kullu' negates the universal affirmative, yielding a particular negative (its contradictory), while 'laysa ba'du' negates the particular affirmative, yielding a universal negative. On the other hand, if we consider them as directly negating the predicate with respect to the subject (i.e., as simple negative propositions), then 'laysa kullu' becomes a universal negative and 'laysa ba'du' a particular negative. Thus each expression can be interpreted as either universal or particular depending on how it is taken. The reason they are conventionally counted among the quantifiers of particularity is that in both interpretations, the particular negative is entailed (since the universal negative also implies the particular negative), and the logicians prefer the certain (the particular) over the doubtful. Finally, the passage notes a difference between 'laysa ba'du' and 'ba'du laysa': the former can sometimes be used for universal negation, as in 'Not some animal is a stone' meaning 'No animal is a stone', due to its ambiguity.

Al-Taftazani's Analysis: Two Interpretations of 'Laysa Kullu' and 'Laysa Ba'du'
Al-Taftazani's detailed verification of the two quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two ways of interpreting 'laysa kullu' and 'laysa ba'du' as described by al-Taftazani, and determine whether they yield universal or particular negation in each context.

Core Idea
A quantifier's logical force changes depending on whether it negates the proposition or just the predicate.

Al-Taftazani (al-Sa'd) provides a deeper analysis of the quantifiers 'laysa kullu' and 'laysa ba'du'. He shows that they can be understood in two ways: either as negating the proposition itself (raising it) or as simply negating the predicate with respect to the subject. Depending on the interpretation, the resulting proposition can be universal or particular. This lesson will guide you through this nuanced distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions analyzed by al-Sa'd
From the passage, locate 'laysa kullu' and 'laysa ba'du'. Note that he does not discuss 'ba'du laysa' in this analysis.
2
First consideration: as negations of the proposition
When these quantifiers are considered as entering upon the proposition itself and denying it, 'laysa kullu' negates the universal affirmative, yielding a particular negative. 'Laysa ba'du' negates the particular affirmative, yielding a universal negative.
3
Second consideration: as negations of the predicate
When they are considered as simply negating the predicate from the subject, 'laysa kullu' becomes a universal negative (no S is P), and 'laysa ba'du' becomes a particular negative (some S is not P).
4
Understand why they are counted among particular quantifiers
The passage explains that in both considerations, the particular negative is always true (since universal implies particular), so they took the certain (the particular) and left the doubtful.

وَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الجُزْئِيّ: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ»، وَ «بَعْضُ لَيْسَ»، وَ «لَيْسَ كُلُّ»؛ نَحْوُ: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ النَّاسِ بِمُحَاسَبٍ»، وَ «بَعْضُ الذَّنْبِ لَيْسَ بِمَغْفُورٍ»، وَ «لَيْسَ كُلُّ مَيْتٍ تَأْكُلُهُ الأَرْضُ»، وَ «مَا جَمِيعُ النَّاسِ بِمُؤْمِنِينَ». وَفَرَّقُوا بَيْنَ هَذِهِ الأَلْفَاظِ: بِأَنَّ «لَيْسَ كُلَّ» مَفْهُومُهُ المُطَابِقِيُّ رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الكُلِّيَّ، وَيَلْزَمُهُ السَّلْبُ الجُزْنِيُّ، وَهُوَ سَلْبُ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ البَعْضِ، وَالآخَرَانِ بِالعَكْسِ، فَإِنَّ مَفْهُومَهُمَا المُطَابِقِيَّ هُوَ السَّلْبُ الجُزْنِيُّ، وَيَلْزَمُهُمَا رَفْعُ الإِيجَابِ الكُلِّيَّ. قَالَ السَّعْدُ: وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ «لَيْسَ كُلَّ» وَ «لَيْسَ بَعْضَ» إِنِ اعْتُبِرَا دَاخِلَيْنِ عَلَى نَفْسِ القَضِيَّةِ رَافِعَيْنِ لَهَا، فَالأَوَّلُ رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الكُلّيّ؛ أَيْ فَيَكُونُ سَلْباً جُزْنِيًّا؛ لِأَنَّهُ نَقِيضُ الإِنْجَابِ الكُلّيّ، وَالثَّانِي: رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الجُزْنِيّ؛ أَيْ: فَيَكُونُ سَلْباً كُلِّيًّا؛ لِأَنَّهُ نَقِيضُ الإِيجَابِ الجُزْئيّ. وَإِنِ اعْتُبِرَا بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى المَحْمُولِ؛ أَيْ: بِأَنْ أُريدَ بِهِمَا سَلْبَ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ المَوْضُوعِ، لَا سَلْبَ القَضِيَّةِ نَفْسِهَا كَمَا فِي الوَجْهِ الأَوَّلِ، فَالأَوَّلُ سَلْبٌ كُلِّيٌّ، وَالثَّانِي سَلْبٌ جُزْئِيٌّ. فَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا يَكُونُ كُلِّيَّا عَلَى تَقْدِيرٍ، وَجُزْيْنًا عَلَى تَقْدِيرٍ، وَإِنَّمَا عُدَّا مِنْ أَسْوَارِ الجُزْنِيَّةِ؛ لأَنَّ الجُزْئِيَّ صَادِقٌ فِي ضُمْنِ الكُلِّيِّ، فَأَخَذُوا المُحَقَّقَ وَتَرَكُوا المَشْكُوكَ. وَالفَرْقُ بَيْنَ «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ» وَ «بَعْضُ لَيْسَ»: أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ قَدْ يُسْتَعْمَلُ لِلسَّلْبِ الكُلّي؛ نَحْوُ: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ بِحَجَرٍ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لِإِبْهَامِهِ.
And the quantifiers of particular negation: 'Not some', and 'Some not', and 'Not all'; such as: 'Not some people are reckonable', and 'Some sin is not forgiven', and 'Not all dead does the earth consume', and 'Not all people are believers'. And they distinguished between these expressions: that 'Not all' has as its immediate meaning the lifting of universal affirmation, and it necessarily entails particular negation, which is the negation of the predicate from some; and the other two are the opposite, for their immediate meaning is particular negation, and they necessarily entail the lifting of universal affirmation. Al-Sa'd said: The verification is that 'Not all' and 'Not some' if they are considered as entering upon the proposition itself, lifting it, then the first is the lifting of universal affirmation; i.e. it becomes particular negation because it is the contradictory of universal affirmation; and the second is the lifting of particular affirmation; i.e. it becomes universal negation because it is the contradictory of particular affirmation. And if they are considered with respect to the predicate, i.e. intending by them the negation of the predicate from the subject, not the negation of the proposition itself as in the first way, then the first is universal negation, and the second is particular negation. So each of them is universal in one estimation and particular in another. They are only counted among the quantifiers of particularity because the particular is true within the universal, so they took the certain and left the doubtful. And the difference between 'Not some' and 'Some not' is that the former may be used for universal negation; such as: 'Not some animal is a stone'; because of its ambiguity.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the quantifiers (sawar) used for particular negative propositions. It begins by listing three expressions: 'laysa ba'du' (not some), 'ba'du laysa' (some not), and 'laysa kullu' (not all). Examples are given from everyday Arabic, such as 'Not some people are reckonable' and 'Some sin is not forgiven'. The author then explains the distinction made by logicians between these expressions in terms of their primary (mutabiqi) meaning and their necessary entailment (iltizam). For 'laysa kullu', the primary meaning is the lifting of universal affirmation (i.e., denying that the predicate applies to all individuals), and it necessarily entails particular negation (that the predicate is denied of some). For the other two, the situation is reversed: their primary meaning is particular negation, and they necessarily entail the lifting of universal affirmation. The passage then quotes al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) who provides a more detailed analysis. He says that if we consider 'laysa kullu' and 'laysa ba'du' as entering upon the proposition itself and denying it (i.e., as negations of the whole proposition), then 'laysa kullu' negates the universal affirmative, yielding a particular negative (its contradictory), while 'laysa ba'du' negates the particular affirmative, yielding a universal negative. On the other hand, if we consider them as directly negating the predicate with respect to the subject (i.e., as simple negative propositions), then 'laysa kullu' becomes a universal negative and 'laysa ba'du' a particular negative. Thus each expression can be interpreted as either universal or particular depending on how it is taken. The reason they are conventionally counted among the quantifiers of particularity is that in both interpretations, the particular negative is entailed (since the universal negative also implies the particular negative), and the logicians prefer the certain (the particular) over the doubtful. Finally, the passage notes a difference between 'laysa ba'du' and 'ba'du laysa': the former can sometimes be used for universal negation, as in 'Not some animal is a stone' meaning 'No animal is a stone', due to its ambiguity.

سور السلب الجزئي
مفهوم مطابقي
رفع الإيجاب الكلي
سلب جزئي

'Laysa kullu' can be either particular or universal depending on whether it negates the proposition or the predicate, but logicians prefer the particular interpretation due to certainty.

Two Interpretations of 'Not All' and 'Not Some'
Sa'd al-Din explains the two ways of interpreting universal and particular negation quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two interpretations of 'not all' and 'not some' and determine the resulting quantity and quality of the proposition.

Core Idea
The same quantifier phrase can be universal or particular depending on what it negates.

This passage from Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani clarifies a subtle but important point in Islamic logic: the quantifiers 'not all' and 'not some' can be read in two different ways. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting negated propositions in classical texts, especially in discussions of contradictories and immediate inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts
Sa'd explains that 'not all' and 'not some' can be considered either as entering upon the entire proposition to negate it (first interpretation) or as directly negating the predicate (second interpretation). Notice the shift in what is being negated.
The first interpretation negates the proposition itself; the second negates the predicate only.
2
Analyze the first interpretation
Under the first interpretation, 'not all' negates the universal affirmative proposition, resulting in a particular negative (contradictory of 'all'). 'Not some' negates the particular affirmative, resulting in a universal negative (contradictory of 'some').
This follows the standard square of opposition: the contradictory of A is O, and of I is E.
3
Analyze the second interpretation
Under the second interpretation, 'not all' is taken as a universal negation of the predicate (e.g., 'All S are not P' = No S is P). 'Not some' becomes a particular negation (e.g., 'Some S are not P').
Here the quantifier attached to the subject (all/some) is preserved, and the negation applies to the predicate.
4
Compare the results
Notice that in the first interpretation, 'not all' yields particular negation, while in the second it yields universal negation. Conversely, 'not some' yields universal negation in the first interpretation and particular negation in the second. Each phrase can be either universal or particular depending on the reading.
This shows that the logical form depends on the scope of negation.
5
Understand the classification
Sa'd explains that these phrases are counted among the 'quantifiers of particularity' because the particular form is true whenever the universal is true (the particular is contained within the universal). Since the particular reading is always valid, they took the certain (particular) and left the doubtful (universal).
This is a pragmatic choice: when in doubt, use the more inclusive (particular) reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ السَّعْدُ : وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ لَيْسَ كُلُّ وَ لَيْسَ بَعْضُ إِنِ اعْتُبِرًا دَاخِلَيْنِ عَلَى نَفْسِ القَضِيَّةِ رَافِعَيْنِ لَهَا ، فَالأَوَّلُ رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الكُلّيّ؛ أَي فَيَكُونُ سَلْباً جُزْئِيًّا؛ لِأَنَّهُ نَقِيضُ الإِيجَابِ الكُلّيّ ، وَالثَّانِي: رَفْعُ الإِيْجَابِ الجُزْئِي؛ أَيْ: فَيَكُونُ سَلْباً كُلِّيًّا؛ لِأَنَّهُ نَقِيضُ الإِيجَابِ الجُزْئيّ . وَإِنِ اعْتُبِرَا بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى المَحْمُولِ ؛ أَيْ : بِأَنْ أُريدَ بِهِمَا سَلْبَ المَحْمُولِ عَنِ المَوْضُوعِ، لَا سَلْبَ القَضِيَّةِ نَفْسِهَا كَمَا فِي الوَجْهِ الأَوَّلِ، فَالأَوَّلُ سَلْبٌ كُلِّيٌّ [ب/ ۳۲]، وَالثَّانِي سَلْبٌ جزئي. فَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا يَكُونُ كُلِّيَّا عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ، وَجُزْئِيًّا عَلَى تَقْدِيرٍ، وَإِنَّمَا عُدَّا مِنْ أَسْوَارِ الجُزْنِيَّةِ ؛ لأَنَّ الجُزئِيَّ صَادِقٌ فِي ضُمْنِ الكُلِّيِّ ، فَأَخَذُوا المُحَقَّقَ وَتَرَكُوا المَشْكُوكَ.
Sa'd said: 'The investigation is that 'not all' and 'not some' if they are considered as entering upon the proposition itself, negating it, then the first is the negation of universal affirmation; i.e., it becomes particular negation because it is the contradictory of universal affirmation. And the second is the negation of particular affirmation; i.e., it becomes universal negation because it is the contradictory of particular affirmation. And if they are considered with respect to the predicate, i.e., by intending by them the negation of the predicate from the subject, not the negation of the proposition itself as in the first way, then the first is universal negation, and the second is particular negation. So each of them is universal on one estimation and particular on another. And they were only counted among the quantifiers of particularity because the particular is true within the universal, so they took the realized and left the doubtful.'
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis by Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, quoted in the commentary on the Sullam al-Munawraq. Sa'd clarifies a subtle point about the quantifiers 'laysa kull' (not all) and 'laysa ba'd' (not some). He explains that these phrases can be interpreted in two ways. First, if they are considered as acting on the entire proposition to negate it, then 'not all' negates universal affirmation, resulting in a particular negation (e.g., 'Not all men are black' means 'Some men are not black'). 'Not some' negates particular affirmation, resulting in a universal negation (e.g., 'Not some men are black' means 'No men are black'). Second, if they are considered as directly negating the predicate, then 'not all' becomes universal negation (e.g., 'All men are not black' means 'No men are black'), and 'not some' becomes particular negation (e.g., 'Some men are not black'). Thus each expression can be either universal or particular depending on the interpretation. The reason they are categorized under 'particular quantifiers' is that the particular case is always true when the universal is true (the particular is included in the universal), so the certain (the particular) is taken and the doubtful (the universal) is left aside. This is a standard logical analysis in the Islamic tradition, focused on precision of quantifier usage.

Key Takeaway
The logical form of 'not all' and 'not some' depends on whether they negate the entire proposition or just the predicate, making each expression potentially universal or particular.
