نَحْرُ : زَيْدٌ هُوَ العَالِمُ»، وَلَيْسَ عَائِداً إِلَى مَا قَبْلَهُ ; إِذْ لَيْسَ بِاسْمِ، قوله : (الغَالِبُ تَرْكُهَا) مفهوم الغالب : أنَّه قد يحتاج إليها، فتذكَّر . قوله : (فِي اللُّغَةِ العَرَبِيَّةِ) لا في غيرها من لغة اليونان والعجم، فلهم بها مزيد اهتمام، فيلتزمونها في كلِّ موضع نيَّة ; سواء ذكرت، أو لم تذكر . قوله : (وَهِيَ فِي لُغَةِ العَرَبِ ضَمِيرُ الفَصْلِ ; نَحْوُ : «زَيْدٌ هُوَ العَالِمُ») فلولا الضَّمير; لتوهم أنَّ العالم صفة لزيد، لا محكوم به عليه، ولا شك أنَّ ضمير الفصل لا يوجد في كلامهم في كلّ محل، وإنَّما يكون بين جزأي الابتداء إذا كانا معرفتين أو نكرتين كمعرفتين في امتناع لحاق «أل». وما أفهمه كلامُ هذا الشَّارح من أنَّ ضمير الفصل موضوع للبط في اللغة العربية حيث يكون صالحاً له، وإلا امتنع واكتفى برابطة الإعراب هو الذي صرّح به التفتازاني في حواشي «الكشاف»; إذ صرَّح بأنَّ ضمير الفصل له فوائد ثلاثة : الحصر، والفصل، والربط. وكذلك العقباني صرّح بذلك، بلِ الظَّاهرُ مِن كلام السَّنوسي : أنَّ العرب وضعت الضمير للربط، وإن لم يكن الموضع موضع ضمير فصل، و به صرح ابن سينا . والذي حرَّره جماعة كابن واصل في شرح الجمل والسعد في شرح الشَّمسيَّة»، والقطب في شرح المطالع : أنَّ العرب لم تضع للربط لفظاً أصلاً ; استغناء برابطة الإعراب ـ أي : حركة الإعراب ، أو ما يقوم مقامها من الحروف بل حركة الرفع تحقيقاً أو تقديراً لا غير; لأنَّا إذا قلنا : زيد عالم على سبيل التّعداد ، لم يفهم منه الربط والإسناد، وإذا قلنا : زيد قائم بالرفع، فُهِم ذلك فالرابطة هي الحركة الإعرابية فقط قال ابن واصل : ضمير الفصل ليس برابطة، وإن اعتقد بعضهم أنَّه رابطةٌ، وإنَّما أتي به في نحو : زيد هو الكاتب» فصلاً بين كون «الكاتب صفة، وبين كونه خبراً . وما ذكره هؤلاء من أنَّ العرب لم تضع للربط لفظاً هو الذي صوّبه شيخنا سيدي الطَّيِّب واستظهره شيخنا ابن منصور، وبه يكون كلامهم جارٍ على أسلوبٍ واحدٍ. قوله: (وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ حَرْفٌ... إلخ) أتى بطريق الحصر ردًّا على الخليل القائل: «بأنَّه اسم»، ونسب أبو حيان في الارتشاف»: القول بحرفيته للأكثر، وصححه ابن عصفور، وذلك لأنَّه موضوع لكلِّ نسبة مخصوصة غير مقصودة لذاتها، بل تتعقل تبعاً للطَّرفين، وهذا معنى الحرف. وعلى القول: «بأنَّه اسم بنى السَّعد قوله في المطوّل»: «لأنَّه - أي: ضمير الفصل - في المعنى عبارة عنه - أي عن المسند إليه ، وفي اللفظ مطابق له»; أي: في الغيبة والحضور والإفراد : والتذكير وفروعهما، وأمَّا على قول الجمهور فلا هو عبارة عنه، ولا له مفسّر، وتسميته ضميراً باعتبار الصُّورة فقط
Then this copula is usually omitted in the Arabic language, dispensing with it by means of declension and the adjacency of the two terms. But it may be needed, so it is mentioned. For this reason - and God knows best - the versifier confined himself to mentioning the two terms. And it is in the language of the Arabs the pronoun of separation, like 'Zayd huwa al-'alim'. It is not referring back to what precedes it, because it is not a noun. He said: (The usual omission) the understanding of 'usual' is that it may be needed, so it is mentioned. He said: (In the Arabic language) not in other languages like Greek and Persian, for they have more interest in it, so they commit to it in every place intended, whether mentioned or not. He said: (And it is in the language of the Arabs the pronoun of separation, like 'Zayd huwa al-alim') For without the pronoun, it would be understood that 'al-alim' is a description of Zayd, not predicated of him. And there is no doubt that the pronoun of separation does not occur in their speech in every place; rather it occurs between the two parts of the subject-predicate sentence when both are definite or both indefinite like definites in that 'al' is not attached. And what I understand from the speech of this commentator that the pronoun of separation is placed for connection in Arabic where it is suitable, otherwise it is impossible and one suffices with the copula of declension. This is what al-Taftazani stated in his glosses on al-Kashshaf, for he stated that the pronoun of separation has three benefits: restriction (hasyr), separation (fasl), and connection (rābiṭ). Likewise al-Uqbānī stated that. Rather, the apparent from the speech of al-Sanūsī is that the Arabs placed the pronoun for connection, even if the place is not a place of pronoun of separation, and Ibn Sīnā stated it. And what a group like Ibn Wāṣil in his commentary on al-Jumal, al-Saʿd in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya, and al-Quṭb in his commentary on al-Muṭāliʿ have verified: that the Arabs did not place a word for connection at all, dispensing with the copula of declension – i.e., the movement of declension, or what stands in its place of letters, but only the movement of rafʿ (nominative) actual or virtual – because if we say 'Zayd ʿālim' in a counting way, connection and predication are not understood; if we say 'Zayd qā'im' with rafʿ, that is understood. So the copula is the declension movement only. Ibn Wāṣil said: The pronoun of separation is not a copula, even if some think it is a copula; rather it is brought in such as 'Zayd huwa al-kātib' as a separation between 'al-kātib' being a description and being a predicate. And what these people mentioned – that the Arabs did not put a word for connection – is the opinion that our teacher Sayyidī al-Ṭayyib deemed correct, and our teacher Ibn Manṣūr favored, and thus their speech proceeds on a single style. He said: (It is only a letter... etc.) He came with a restrictive way in refutation of al-Khalīl who said it is a noun, and Abū Ḥayyān in al-Irtishāf attributed the opinion of its being a letter to the majority, and Ibn ʿUṣfūr corrected it. That is because it is placed for a specific relation not intended for itself, rather it is understood subsidiary to the two terms, and this is the meaning of a letter. And according to the opinion that it is a noun, al-Saʿd based his statement in al-Muṭawwal: 'Because it – meaning the pronoun of separation – in meaning is an expression of it – meaning the subject – and in pronunciation is agreeing with it' i.e., in gender, presence, singularity, and their branches. And on the opinion of the majority, it is not an expression of it, nor does it have an explicative, and its being called a pronoun is only with respect to form.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage focuses on the copula (al-rabita) in Arabic. The author begins by noting that in the Arabic language, the copula is generally omitted because the declension (i'rab) and the juxtaposition of the subject and predicate suffice to indicate the connection. However, occasionally the copula is needed and thus mentioned. The versifier of al-Sullam only mentions the two terms (subject and predicate) for this reason. The copula in Arabic takes the form of the pronoun of separation (damir al-fasl), as in 'Zayd huwa al-'alim'. This pronoun is not a true pronoun referring to what precedes; instead it is a letter (harf) used for a specific purpose. The commentator then refines this: 'usual omission' implies that it can be needed. He also notes that in non-Arabic languages like Greek and Persian, the copula is always used. The pronoun of separation serves to prevent misunderstanding: without it, one might think that 'al-'alim' is a description (sifa) of Zayd rather than a predication (khabar). The pronoun of separation is not used in every context; it is used specifically when both subject and predicate are definite (or both indefinite resembling definites) and when joining with 'al' is not possible. The commentator then discusses the views of various scholars: al-Taftazani in his glosses on al-Kashshaf says the pronoun of separation has three benefits: restriction (al-hasr), separation (al-fasl), and connection (al-rabi). Others like al-Sanusi and Ibn Sina hold that the pronoun was placed specifically for connection. However, a group of scholars including Ibn Wasil, al-Sa'd al-Taftazani, and al-Qutb al-Razi have determined that the Arabs did not assign a word specifically for the copula; rather, the copula is simply the movement of declension (harakat al-i'rab), specifically the nominative (raf') movement, either actual or virtual. For example, saying 'Zayd 'alim' without any linking word, the nominative ending conveys the predication. Thus, the pronoun of separation is not a copula but a separator between a description and a predicate. The author concludes that this latter view is the one adopted by his teachers and is consistent with the general Arabic style. Finally, a grammatical point: the pronoun of separation is a letter (harf) according to the majority, refuting al-Khalil who said it is a noun. Abu Hayyan attributed the letter view to the majority, and Ibn 'Usfur confirmed it. The criterion for a letter is that it signifies a specific relation not intended for itself but understood in dependence on the two terms. On the noun view, al-Sa'd al-Taftazani built his statement that the pronoun in meaning expresses the subject and in form agrees with it. On the majority view, it is neither an expression nor has an explicative, and is called 'pronoun' only formally.

Key Terms
ضمير الفصل
الحرف
الاسم
الخليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) To separate between a description and a predicate
C) To indicate the definite article

Key Takeaway
The pronoun of separation is a separator, not the copula, and the majority classifies it as a letter (harf) because it signifies a relation not intended for itself.

The True Copula in Arabic: Inflectional Vowel, Not a Word
Author argues that the copula 'huwa' is a particle and refines the copula concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true copula (raabita) in Arabic categorical propositions and explain why 'huwa' is not a copula but a separator.

Core Idea
The Arabic copula is not a word—it is the inflectional vowel itself.

Grammarians and logicians debated whether the word 'huwa' functions as a copula in propositions like 'Zayd huwa kātib'. The author argues that 'huwa' is a particle, not a noun, and presents a refined view that the Arabs originally used no word for the copula; instead, the inflectional vowel (i'rāb) alone serves to connect subject and predicate. This lesson walks through the evidence and the scholarly refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under discussion
Locate the word 'huwa' in the Arabic passage and note that it is used between subject and predicate in logical propositions. The author refers to it as a particle (harf).
In 'Zayd huwa kātib', 'huwa' appears as a separating pronoun.
2
Understand the two views
Al-Khalil claimed 'huwa' is a noun; the majority (as cited by Abu Hayyan and Ibn Asfur) said it is a particle. Al-Sa'd built on the noun view, but the particle view is defended here.
The particle view is based on the idea that 'huwa' signifies a relation not intended by itself.
3
Examine the author's rebuttal of al-Khalil
The author uses restriction ('innamā') to refute al-Khalil, asserting that 'huwa' is a particle. The evidence: it is posited for a specific relation (predication) and is not self-standing.
This sets up the refined analysis.
4
Analyze the evidence from Arab usage
Scholars like Ibn Wasil, al-Sa'd, and al-Qutb argued that the Arabs used no word for the copula. Instead, the inflectional vowel (specifically the nominative case) indicates predication. Example: 'Zayd qā'im' with nominative on 'qā'im' conveys the judgment, while 'Zayd 'ālim' in listing does not.
Thus the true copula (raabita) is the inflectional vowel, not a separate word.
5
Recognize the function of 'huwa' as a separator
Ibn Wasil explains that 'huwa' is not a copula but a separator (damir al-fasl) used to distinguish predicate from adjective. In 'Zayd huwa al-kātib', it prevents 'al-kātib' from being interpreted as an adjective.
Hence 'huwa' is a particle for separation, not for predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ حَرْفٌ جِيْءَ بِهِ لِمَعْنٍ فِي غَيْرِهِ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ مَا بَعْدَهُ مَحْكُومٌ بِهِ عَلَى مَا قَبْلَهُ، لَا صِفَةٌ لَهُ، وَقَدْ تَوَسَّعَ المَنْطِقِيُّونَ فَاسْتَعْمَلُوا لَفْظَةَ هُوَ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لِلدَّلَالَةِ عَلَى الحُكْمِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنِ المَوْضُوعُ صَالِحاً لِضَمِيرِ الفَصْلِ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ هُوَ كَاتِبٌ . قوله: (وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ حَرْفٌ... إلخ) أتى بطريق الحصر ردًّا على الخليل القائل: «بأنَّه اسم»، ونسب أبو حيان في الارتشاف: القول بحرفيته للأكثر، وصححه ابن عصفور، وذلك لأنَّه موضوع لكلِّ نسبة مخصوصة غير مقصودة لذاتها، بل تتعقل تبعاً للطَّرفين، وهذا معنى الحرف. وعلى القول: «بأنَّه اسم بنى السَّعد قوله في المطوّل: «لأنَّه - أي: ضمير الفصل - في المعنى عبارة عنه - أي عن المسند إليه، وفي اللفظ مطابق له»؛ أي: في الغيبة والحضور والإفراد والتذكير وفروعهما، وأمَّا على قول الجمهور فلا هو عبارة عنه، ولا له مفسّر، وتسميته ضميراً باعتبار الصُّورة فقط. والذي حرَّره جماعة كابن واصل في شرح الجمل والسعد في شرح الشمسيَّة، والقطب في شرح المطالع: أنَّ العرب لم تضع للربط لفظاً أصلاً؛ استغناء برابطة الإعراب ـ أي: حركة الإعراب، أو ما يقوم مقامها من الحروف بل حركة الرفع تحقيقاً أو تقديراً لا غير؛ لأنَّا إذا قلنا : زيد عالم على سبيل التعداد، لم يفهم منه الربط والإسناد، وإذا قلنا : زيد قائم بالرفع، فُهِم ذلك فالرابطة هي الحركة الإعرابية فقط قال ابن واصل : ضمير الفصل ليس برابطة، وإن اعتقد بعضهم أنَّه رابطةٌ، وإنَّما أتي به في نحو : زيد هو الكاتب فصلاً بين كون الكاتب صفة، وبين كونه خبراً. وما ذكره هؤلاء من أنَّ العرب لم تضع للربط لفظاً هو الذي صوّبه شيخنا سيدي الطَّيِّب واستظهره شيخنا ابن منصور، وبه يكون كلامهم جارٍ على أسلوبٍ واحدٍ.
Rather it is a particle brought for a meaning in other than itself, namely that what comes after it is predicated of what comes before it, not a description. And the logicians expanded [its usage] and used the word 'huwa' between the two terms to indicate the judgment, even if the subject is not suitable for a separating pronoun, e.g., 'Zayd huwa a writer.' His saying: ('Rather it is a particle... etc.') He presented it in the style of restriction in refutation of al-Khalil who said it is a noun. Abu Hayyan in al-Irtishaf attributed the view that it is a particle to the majority, and Ibn Asfur corrected this, because it is posited for every specific relation not intended by itself, but is conceived accidentally with the two terms, and this is the meaning of a particle. According to the view that it is a noun, al-Sa'd based his words in al-Mutawwal: 'Because it—i.e., the separating pronoun—in meaning is an expression of it—i.e., the subject—and in wording corresponds to it'; i.e., in person, presence, number, gender and their branches. But according to the majority view, it is neither an expression of it nor does it have an explanatory phrase, and it is called a pronoun only by form. What a group determined—like Ibn Wasil in Sharh al-Jumal, al-Sa'd in Sharh al-Shamsiyya, and al-Qutb in Sharh al-Mutali'—is that the Arabs originally did not put a word for the copula at all, dispensing with the copula of inflection—i.e., the inflectional vowel, or what takes its place from letters, only the nominative vowel actually or virtually, no other. Because if we say 'Zayd 'alim' in a listing manner, the connection and predication are not understood, and if we say 'Zayd qa'im' with the nominative, it is understood. So the copula is only the inflectional vowel. Ibn Wasil said: The separating pronoun is not a copula, even if some believe it is a copula; rather it is brought in 'Zayd huwa al-katib' to separate between 'al-katib' being an adjective and being a predicate. And what these people mentioned—that the Arabs did not put a word for the copula—is what our teacher Sayyidi al-Tayyib corrected and our teacher Ibn Mansour deemed most apparent, and thus their speech runs on one style.
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the nature of the copula word 'huwa' in Arabic logical propositions. The author begins by stating that 'huwa' is a particle (harf), not a noun, used to link the subject and predicate. It indicates that what follows is predicated of what precedes, not as a description but as a judgment. Logicians extended its use even when the context does not require a separating pronoun, as in 'Zayd huwa writer.' The commentary then explains that this is a rebuttal of al-Khalil, who claimed it is a noun. Abu Hayyan attributes the particle view to the majority, and Ibn Asfur corrects it. The rationale is that a particle signifies a relation not intended by itself. Al-Sa'd's view that it is a noun is then discussed: he built on the idea that the separating pronoun in meaning corresponds to the subject and agrees with it in person, number, etc. However, the majority disagree. Next, the author presents the view of several scholars (Ibn Wasil, al-Sa'd, al-Qutb) that the Arabs originally had no explicit copula word; they relied solely on inflectional vowels (i'rāb) to indicate predication. For example, 'Zayd qā'im' with nominative vowel conveys predication, while 'Zayd 'ālim' without inflection would not. Thus the true copula is the inflectional vowel. The separating pronoun 'huwa' is not a copula but a separator to distinguish predicate from adjective. The author concludes that this refined view is accepted by his teachers.

ضمير الفصل
رابطة

Key Takeaway
The Arabs did not use a word for the copula; the inflectional vowel (i'rāb) alone serves to connect subject and predicate, and 'huwa' is only a separator (damir al-fasl), not a copula.

Binary and Ternary Propositions: The Role of the Copula
Author defines proposition types based on presence of copula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between binary and ternary propositions based on the presence of an explicit copula, and identify the copula in a given proposition.

Core Idea
The presence or absence of an explicit copula changes a proposition's classification from binary to ternary.

In Islamic logic, the structure of a proposition is crucial. This lesson introduces the classification of propositions as binary (thuna'iyya) or ternary (thulathiyya) based on whether the copula (raabita) is explicitly mentioned. Understanding this distinction is the first step to analyzing the internal structure of judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition
Find the subject and predicate in the Arabic proposition. In a proposition like 'العالم حادث', 'العالم' is subject and 'حادث' is predicate.
2
Look for an explicit copula
Check if there is a word like 'هو' linking subject and predicate. In 'العالم هو حادث', 'هو' appears; in 'العالم حادث', it does not.
The copula may be omitted in Arabic, relying on inflection.
3
Classify as binary or ternary
If no explicit copula, the proposition is binary (ثنائية). If an explicit copula is present, it is ternary (ثلاثية).
The terms refer to the number of explicit words: subject, predicate, and optionally copula.
4
Understand the copula's role
The copula indicates the relation between subject and predicate. In ternary propositions, the copula makes the judgment explicit, which is useful for formal logical analysis.
Non-Arabic languages have dedicated copulas; Arabic borrowed 'huwa' for this function.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها وَأَمَّا غَيْرُ العَرَبِ، فَلَهُمْ أَلْفَاظٌ أُخَرُ تَكُونُ رَابِطَةً، وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ عِنْدَ حَذْفِ الرَّابِطَةِ: ثُنَائِيَّةً، وَعِنْدَ ذِكْرِهَا: ثُلَاثِيَّةً. قوله: (وَأَمَّا غَيْرُ العَرَبِ، فَلَهُمْ أَلْفَاظُ أُخَرُ ... إلخ) المستعمل عند الفلاسفة أصالة في الربط: «هست» في الفارسية، و: «استبر» في اليونانية. ولما احتاج الفلاسفة الذين يتكلمون بالعربيَّة أوّل الوضع لفظاً يقوم مقام ذلك، التمسوا في لغة العرب لفظاً ينقلونه إلى ذلك، فاختار بعضهم لفظة: «هو»؛ لأنَّها قد تستعمل في بعض الأمكنة التي تستعمل فيها لفظة: «هست»؛ كما في قولنا: «هذا هو زيد، وهذا هو الشّاعر».
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. As for non-Arabs, they have other words that serve as a copula. The proposition when the copula is omitted is called binary, and when it is mentioned is called ternary. His saying: (As for non-Arabs, they have other words... etc.) The term used originally by philosophers for the copula is 'hast' in Persian and 'estabr' in Greek. When Arabic-speaking philosophers first needed a word to serve that function, they sought in the Arabic language a word to transfer to that meaning. Some chose the word 'huwa' because it is sometimes used in places where 'hast' is used, as in our saying: 'This is Zayd, this is the poet.'
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage provides a definitional classification of propositions based on the presence or absence of an explicit copula. The author states that a proposition without a copula is called 'binary' (thuna'iyya), and with a copula, 'ternary' (thulathiyya). The commentary explains that non-Arabic languages like Persian and Greek have dedicated words for the copula ('hast' and 'estabr' respectively). Arabic originally lacked such a word, but when Arabic-speaking philosophers needed one, they borrowed the Arabic word 'huwa' because it appears in constructions like 'This is Zayd' where it functions similarly. Thus the classification is linked to the history of philosophical terminology.

Propositions
beginner
8 min
Semantics of the Subject: Individuals vs. Concept
Author clarifies subject's intended meaning is its individuals, with natural universal exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the subject of a universal proposition is taken extensionally (masduq) or intensionally (mafhum) based on quantifiers and the natural universal exception.

Core Idea
The subject of a quantified proposition normally refers to its individuals, not its concept.

This lesson clarifies a foundational semantic rule in Islamic logic: what exactly does the subject of a proposition like 'every human is rational' refer to? The author explains that normally the subject refers to its individuals (masduq), as shown by the use of quantifiers. However, when the subject is the natural universal (al-tabi'iyya), the concept itself is intended. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting categorical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Check for quantifiers
If the proposition has a quantifier like 'every' or 'some', that indicates the subject is probably taken extensionally. Quantifiers apply to individuals, not concepts.
The passage uses 'acceptance of quantifiers' as evidence.
2
Identify natural universal
If the subject is a natural universal (tabi'iyya), it is taken intensionally. The natural universal refers to the essence alone without regard to individuals.
The exception is explicitly stated: 'except for the natural universal'.
3
Apply the multiplicity test
Can the subject be meaningfully said to have multiple instances? If yes, it is extensional; if not, it is intensional. For 'every human is an animal', 'human' has many individuals like Zayd and Amr, so it is extensional.
The passage argues that if we intended the concept, 'every' could not apply because the concept is one.
4
Recognize the default rule
Unless specified as natural universal, always take the subject by its individuals in quantified propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهات: الأَوَّلُ: المَقْصُودُ مِنَ المَوْضُوعِ مَصْدُوقُهُ؛ أَيْ: أَفْرَادُهُ؛ بِدَلِيلِ قَبُولِ السُّوْرِ، إِلَّا الطَّبِيعِيَّةَ فَيُقْصَدُ بِهِ فِيهَا مَفْهُومُهُ، وَالمُرَادُ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ مَفْهُومُهُ، لَا مَصْدُوقُهُ؛ هَذَا هُوَ المَعْرُوفُ وَالمُتَعَيِّنُ. قوله: (مَصْدُوقُهُ؛ أَيْ: أَفْرَادُهُ) اعلم: أنَّ الألفاظ المستعملة عندهم هنا: المصدوق، والماصدق، والذات؛ وكلُّها بمعنى واحدٍ؛ أي: الأفراد التي يصدقُ عليها الشَّيء، فمصدوق الموضوع وماصدقاتُهُ وذاتُهُ: أفراده كـ «زيد، وعمرو» في: «كل إنسان كاتب». والماصدقُ: بعضهم ينطق به ساكن الصَّاد مجموعاً فيه الساكنان على غير حد الجمع، وبعضهم بفتحها وهو منقول من «ما» الموصولة الاسمية وصنتها؛ إذ أصلُه: «ما صدق عليه الشَّيء». قوله: (بِدَلِيلِ قَبُولِ السُّوْرِ) فلو قصدنا بإنسان في قولنا: كلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان الحقيقة من حيث هي؛ أعني: المفهوم الذي هو الحيوان الناطق، لم يصح دخول كل عليه؛ إذ الحقيقة من حيث هي شيء واحد لا تعدُّد فيه، ويلزم أيضاً: أن لا يصدق «كل إنسان شخص». قوله: (وَالمُرَادُ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ مَفْهُومُهُ) أي: لأنا لو أردنا الماصدق: فإما أن يكون مصدوقه غير مصدوق الموضوع، فيبطل الحمل للكذب؛ لأنَّ أفراد «الحيوان» في قولنا: كلُّ إنسان حيوان مباينةٌ لأفراد الإنسان؛ لصدقها بأفراد الإنسان وغيره من العجماوات. وإن كان مصدوقه هو مصدوق الموضوع، لزم انتفاء الفائدة في الحمل، فإذا أردنا بـ «إنسان» في قولنا: كلُّ كاتب إنسان: زيد وعمرو ونحوهما صدقاً، قلنا: زيد وعمرو وخالد ونحوهم هم زيد وعمرو وخالد ونحوهم، وذلك خال عن الفائدة.
Notices: The first: The intended meaning of the subject is its referent (masduq), i.e., its individuals, as evidenced by the acceptance of quantifiers, except for the natural universal (tabi'iyya) in which case its concept is intended. And the intended meaning of the predicate is its concept, not its referent. This is the known and determined view. His saying: (its referent, i.e., its individuals) Know that the terms used here are: al-masduq, al-masadiq, and al-dhat; all have the same meaning, i.e., the individuals of which the thing is true. So the masduq of the subject, its masadiq, and its dhat are its individuals, like 'Zayd' and 'Amr' in 'Every human is a writer'. As for al-masadiq, some pronounce it with a sukun on the sad, combining two sukun against the pattern of plural, and some with a fatha, and it is derived from the relative pronoun 'ma' and its particle; its original is 'ma sadaqa 'alayhi al-shay'. His saying: (as evidenced by the acceptance of quantifiers) For if by 'human' in 'Every human is an animal' we intended the quiddity as such, i.e., the concept 'rational animal', it would not be correct to apply 'every' because the quiddity as such is one thing with no multiplicity, and then 'every human is a person' would not be true. His saying: (and the intended meaning of the predicate is its concept) i.e., because if we intended the referent, then either its referent is different from the referent of the subject, in which case the predication would be false because the individuals of 'animal' in 'Every human is an animal' are distinct from the individuals of 'human', since 'animal' is true of humans and other beasts; or if its referent is the same as the referent of the subject, then predication would be pointless, for if by 'human' in 'Every writer is human' we meant Zayd, Amr, etc., then we say 'Zayd, Amr, Khalid, etc. are Zayd, Amr, Khalid, etc.', which is devoid of benefit.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the semantics of subject and predicate in quantified propositions. The author states that the intended meaning of the subject is its individuals (masduq), as shown by the fact that quantifiers like 'every' apply to them. The exception is the 'natural universal' (al-tabi'iyya), where the concept itself is intended. For the predicate, the intended meaning is always its concept, not its individuals. The commentary then explains the terminology: 'masduq', 'masadiq', and 'dhat' all refer to the individuals of which a term is true. With an example, 'Every human is a writer', the masduq of 'human' is Zayd, Amr, etc. The reason for this is given: if we intended the quiddity (concept) of human in 'Every human is an animal', then the quantifier 'every' could not apply because the quiddity is one thing without multiplicity. Additionally, if the predicate's intended meaning were its individuals, then either the individuals differ (making the proposition false, as 'animal' includes non-humans) or they are identical (making the proposition trivial, like saying all writers are themselves). Therefore, the predicate must be taken by concept to avoid falsity or triviality.

Key Takeaway
The subject of a quantified proposition is normally taken extensionally (masduq) unless it is the natural universal (tabi'iyya).
